"""KDA forward solution with custom CUDA and Triton kernels."""
from __future__ import annotations

import torch
import torch.nn as nn
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline
import triton
import triton.language as tl

# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
# CUDA forward substitution kernel (loaded inline)
# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
_CUDA_FWD_SUBST_SRC = """
__global__ void fwd_subst_kernel(float* __restrict__ A, int C, int batch) {
    int b = blockIdx.x;
    if (b >= batch) return;
    int tid = threadIdx.x;
    __shared__ float s_A[64][64];
    __shared__ float s_row[64];
    float* mat = A + b * C * C;
    for (int idx = tid; idx < C * C; idx += blockDim.x) {
        s_A[idx / C][idx % C] = mat[idx];
    }
    __syncthreads();
    for (int i = 1; i < C; i++) {
        if (tid < i) s_row[tid] = s_A[i][tid];
        __syncthreads();
        if (tid < i) {
            float sum = 0.0f;
            for (int k = 0; k < i; k++) {
                sum += s_row[k] * s_A[k][tid];
            }
            s_A[i][tid] += sum;
        }
        __syncthreads();
    }
    for (int idx = tid; idx < C * C; idx += blockDim.x) {
        mat[idx] = s_A[idx / C][idx % C];
    }
}
torch::Tensor fwd_subst(torch::Tensor A) {
    int batch = A.size(0);
    int C = A.size(1);
    fwd_subst_kernel<<<batch, 64>>>(A.data_ptr<float>(), C, batch);
    return A;
}
"""

_fwd_subst_mod = load_inline(
    name="kda_fwd_subst_v2",
    cpp_sources="torch::Tensor fwd_subst(torch::Tensor A);",
    cuda_sources=_CUDA_FWD_SUBST_SRC,
    functions=["fwd_subst"],
    extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3"],
    verbose=False,
)


# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
# Triton kernel: inter-chunk recurrence + output
# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
@triton.jit
def kda_inter_chunk_kernel(
    q_ptr,
    k_ptr,
    u_ptr,
    w_ptr,
    g_ptr,
    Aqk_ptr,
    o_ptr,
    B: tl.constexpr,
    T: tl.constexpr,
    H: tl.constexpr,
    K: tl.constexpr,
    V: tl.constexpr,
    BT: tl.constexpr,
    BV: tl.constexpr,
    scale: tl.constexpr,
):
    i_v = tl.program_id(0)
    i_bh = tl.program_id(1)
    i_b = i_bh // H
    i_h = i_bh % H

    NT = T // BT

    b_S1 = tl.zeros((64, BV), dtype=tl.float32)
    b_S2 = tl.zeros((64, BV), dtype=tl.float32)

    q_base = q_ptr + (i_b * T * H + i_h) * K
    k_base = k_ptr + (i_b * T * H + i_h) * K
    u_base = u_ptr + (i_b * T * H + i_h) * V
    w_base = w_ptr + (i_b * T * H + i_h) * K
    g_base = g_ptr + (i_b * T * H + i_h) * K
    Aqk_base = Aqk_ptr + (i_b * T * H + i_h) * BT
    o_base = o_ptr + (i_b * T * H + i_h) * V

    for i_t in range(NT):
        t0 = i_t * BT

        p_u = tl.make_block_ptr(
            u_base, (T, V), (H * V, 1), (t0, i_v * BV), (BT, BV), (1, 0)
        )
        b_v = tl.load(p_u, boundary_check=(0, 1)).to(tl.float32)

        p_w = tl.make_block_ptr(
            w_base, (T, K), (H * K, 1), (t0, 0), (BT, 64), (1, 0)
        )
        b_w = tl.load(p_w, boundary_check=(0, 1))
        b_v -= tl.dot(b_w, b_S1.to(b_w.dtype))

        p_w = tl.make_block_ptr(
            w_base, (T, K), (H * K, 1), (t0, 64), (BT, 64), (1, 0)
        )
        b_w = tl.load(p_w, boundary_check=(0, 1))
        b_v -= tl.dot(b_w, b_S2.to(b_w.dtype))

        b_o = tl.zeros((BT, BV), dtype=tl.float32)

        p_q = tl.make_block_ptr(
            q_base, (T, K), (H * K, 1), (t0, 0), (BT, 64), (1, 0)
        )
        p_g = tl.make_block_ptr(
            g_base, (T, K), (H * K, 1), (t0, 0), (BT, 64), (1, 0)
        )
        b_q = tl.load(p_q, boundary_check=(0, 1))
        b_g = tl.load(p_g, boundary_check=(0, 1)).to(tl.float32)
        b_qg = (b_q * tl.exp(b_g)).to(b_q.dtype)
        b_o += tl.dot(b_qg, b_S1.to(b_qg.dtype))

        p_q = tl.make_block_ptr(
            q_base, (T, K), (H * K, 1), (t0, 64), (BT, 64), (1, 0)
        )
        p_g = tl.make_block_ptr(
            g_base, (T, K), (H * K, 1), (t0, 64), (BT, 64), (1, 0)
        )
        b_q = tl.load(p_q, boundary_check=(0, 1))
        b_g = tl.load(p_g, boundary_check=(0, 1)).to(tl.float32)
        b_qg = (b_q * tl.exp(b_g)).to(b_q.dtype)
        b_o += tl.dot(b_qg, b_S2.to(b_qg.dtype))

        b_o *= scale

        p_Aqk = tl.make_block_ptr(
            Aqk_base, (T, BT), (H * BT, 1), (t0, 0), (BT, BT), (1, 0)
        )
        b_Aqk = tl.load(p_Aqk, boundary_check=(0, 1))
        o_i = tl.arange(0, BT)
        m_s = o_i[:, None] >= o_i[None, :]
        b_Aqk = tl.where(m_s, b_Aqk, 0.0).to(b_v.dtype)
        b_o += tl.dot(b_Aqk, b_v.to(b_Aqk.dtype))

        p_o = tl.make_block_ptr(
            o_base, (T, V), (H * V, 1), (t0, i_v * BV), (BT, BV), (1, 0)
        )
        tl.store(p_o, b_o.to(o_ptr.dtype.element_ty), boundary_check=(0, 1))

        last_idx = t0 + BT - 1
        p_glast1 = g_base + last_idx * H * K + tl.arange(0, 64)
        p_glast2 = g_base + last_idx * H * K + 64 + tl.arange(0, 64)
        b_glast1 = tl.load(p_glast1, mask=tl.arange(0, 64) < 64, other=0.0).to(tl.float32)
        b_glast2 = tl.load(p_glast2, mask=tl.arange(0, 64) < 64, other=0.0).to(tl.float32)
        b_decay1 = tl.exp(b_glast1)
        b_decay2 = tl.exp(b_glast2)
        b_S1 *= b_decay1[:, None]
        b_S2 *= b_decay2[:, None]

        p_k = tl.make_block_ptr(
            k_base, (T, K), (H * K, 1), (t0, 0), (BT, 64), (1, 0)
        )
        p_g = tl.make_block_ptr(
            g_base, (T, K), (H * K, 1), (t0, 0), (BT, 64), (1, 0)
        )
        b_k = tl.load(p_k, boundary_check=(0, 1))
        b_g = tl.load(p_g, boundary_check=(0, 1)).to(tl.float32)
        b_kg = b_k * tl.exp(b_glast1[None, :] - b_g)
        b_S1 += tl.dot(b_kg.to(b_v.dtype).T, b_v.to(b_k.dtype))

        p_k = tl.make_block_ptr(
            k_base, (T, K), (H * K, 1), (t0, 64), (BT, 64), (1, 0)
        )
        p_g = tl.make_block_ptr(
            g_base, (T, K), (H * K, 1), (t0, 64), (BT, 64), (1, 0)
        )
        b_k = tl.load(p_k, boundary_check=(0, 1))
        b_g = tl.load(p_g, boundary_check=(0, 1)).to(tl.float32)
        b_kg = b_k * tl.exp(b_glast2[None, :] - b_g)
        b_S2 += tl.dot(b_kg.to(b_v.dtype).T, b_v.to(b_k.dtype))


# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
# Model
# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, B: int, T: int, H: int, K: int, V: int, chunk_size: int = 64):
        super().__init__()
        self.B, self.T, self.H, self.K, self.V = B, T, H, K, V
        self.chunk_size = chunk_size
        self.scale = float(K) ** -0.5
        self.register_buffer("_dummy", torch.zeros(1), persistent=False)

    def forward(
        self,
        q: torch.Tensor,
        k: torch.Tensor,
        v: torch.Tensor,
        g: torch.Tensor,
        beta: torch.Tensor,
    ) -> torch.Tensor:
        dtype = v.dtype
        B, T, H, K = q.shape
        V = v.shape[-1]
        BT = self.chunk_size
        assert T % BT == 0
        NT = T // BT

        qf, kf, vf, gf, bf = (x.to(torch.float32) for x in (q, k, v, g, beta))
        qf = qf * self.scale

        # Chunked layout contiguous
        qf = qf.view(B, NT, BT, H, K).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4).contiguous()
        kf = kf.view(B, NT, BT, H, K).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4).contiguous()
        vf = vf.view(B, NT, BT, H, V).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4).contiguous()
        gf = gf.view(B, NT, BT, H, K).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4).contiguous()
        bf = bf.view(B, NT, BT, H).permute(0, 3, 1, 2).contiguous()

        gf = gf.cumsum(-2)

        # ---- Intra-chunk A_kk ----
        kg = kf * gf.exp()
        kgn = kf * (-gf).exp()

        BHN = B * H * NT
        kg_flat = kg.reshape(BHN, BT, K)
        kgn_flat = kgn.reshape(BHN, BT, K)

        A = torch.bmm(kg_flat, kgn_flat.transpose(-2, -1))
        A = A.view(B, H, NT, BT, BT)

        mask_diag_upper = torch.triu(torch.ones(BT, BT, dtype=torch.bool, device=q.device), diagonal=0)
        A = A * bf[..., None]
        A = -A.masked_fill(mask_diag_upper, 0)

        A_flat = A.view(BHN, BT, BT)
        A_flat = _fwd_subst_mod.fwd_subst(A_flat)
        A = A_flat.view(B, H, NT, BT, BT)

        I = torch.eye(BT, dtype=torch.float32, device=q.device)
        A = (A + I) * bf[..., None, :]

        w = torch.matmul(A, (gf.exp() * kf))
        u = torch.matmul(A, vf)

        qg = qf * gf.exp()
        qg_flat = qg.reshape(BHN, BT, K)
        Aqk = torch.bmm(qg_flat, kgn_flat.transpose(-2, -1))
        Aqk = Aqk.view(B, H, NT, BT, BT)
        mask_strict = torch.triu(torch.ones(BT, BT, dtype=torch.bool, device=q.device), diagonal=1)
        Aqk = Aqk.masked_fill(mask_strict, 0)

        # ---- Inter-chunk recurrence via Triton kernel ----
        # Use original (B, T, H, K/V) layout for the kernel to avoid extra copies
        # qf_flat is already standard layout after permute+reshape
        qf_flat = qf.permute(0, 2, 3, 4, 1).reshape(B, T, H, K)
        kf_flat = kf.permute(0, 2, 3, 4, 1).reshape(B, T, H, K)
        u_flat = u.permute(0, 2, 3, 4, 1).reshape(B, T, H, V)
        w_flat = w.permute(0, 2, 3, 4, 1).reshape(B, T, H, K)
        gf_flat = gf.permute(0, 2, 3, 4, 1).reshape(B, T, H, K)
        Aqk_flat = Aqk.permute(0, 2, 3, 4, 1).reshape(B, T, H, BT)
        o = torch.empty_like(v)

        BV = 32
        grid = (triton.cdiv(V, BV), B * H)
        kda_inter_chunk_kernel[grid](
            qf_flat,
            kf_flat,
            u_flat,
            w_flat,
            gf_flat,
            Aqk_flat,
            o,
            B=B,
            T=T,
            H=H,
            K=K,
            V=V,
            BT=BT,
            BV=BV,
            scale=self.scale,
            num_warps=4,
            num_stages=1,
        )

        return o.to(dtype)


# Module-level shape shims
B = 2
T = 1024
H = 8
K = 128
V = 128
CHUNK_SIZE = 64


def get_inputs():
    torch.manual_seed(0)
    q = torch.randn(B, T, H, K, dtype=torch.bfloat16) * 0.1
    k = torch.randn(B, T, H, K, dtype=torch.bfloat16) * 0.1
    v = torch.randn(B, T, H, V, dtype=torch.bfloat16) * 0.1
    g = (torch.randn(B, T, H, K, dtype=torch.float32) * 0.1 - 0.05)
    beta = torch.sigmoid(torch.randn(B, T, H, dtype=torch.bfloat16))
    return [q, k, v, g, beta]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [B, T, H, K, V, CHUNK_SIZE]

<system>54 lines read from file starting from line 1. Total lines in file: 54. End of file reached.</system>
     1	name: 02_kda_cutlass
     2	display_name: "Kimi Delta Attention (chunk forward) via CUTLASS CuTe"
     3	precision: bf16
     4	regime: compute
     5	
     6	# Approximate FLOPs per forward call. KDA forward (chunk form) breakdown per
     7	# token across all heads:
     8	#   - intra-chunk Aqk (q @ k.T with decay): 2*BT*K
     9	#   - intra-chunk Akk + cumulative invert : ~3*BT*K + BT^2
    10	#   - w = A @ (g.exp()*k):                  2*BT*K
    11	#   - u = A @ v:                            2*BT*V
    12	#   - inter-chunk q @ S:                    2*K*V
    13	#   - inter-chunk (Aqk @ v_i):              2*BT*V
    14	#   - state update S += k.T @ v_i:          2*K*V
    15	# Aggregating with a constant of 4 (typical for chunked linear attention) gives
    16	# the formula below. Used only for roofline reporting -- not graded directly.
    17	flops_formula: "4 * B * T * H * (K*V + CHUNK_SIZE*K + CHUNK_SIZE*V)"
    18

Let me start by understanding the algorithm very carefully from the reference. ### Algorithm Breakdown (from reference.py) **Inputs**: q, k (B, T, H, K); v (B, T, H, V); g (B, T, H, K); beta (B, T, H); scale **Phase 1: Preprocessing** - Convert to float32 - q = q * scale - Reshape to (B, H, N, C, D) where C=64, N=T/64 - g = g.cumsum(-2) # cumsum within each chunk **Phase 2: Build A_kk (intra-chunk K-K interaction)** ```python A = zeros(B, H, N, C, C) for i in range(C): k_i = k[..., i, :] # (B, H, N, K) g_i = g[..., i:i+1, :] # (B, H, N, 1, K) A[..., i] = einsum("...cd, ...d -> ...c", k * exp(g - g_i), k_i) A = A * beta[..., None] # beta shape (B, H, N, C) -> (B, H, N, C, 1) A = -A.masked_fill(mask_diag_upper, 0) # lower triangular, no diagonal # Forward substitution for i in range(1, C): A[..., i, :i] = A[..., i, :i] + sum_j(A[..., i, j] * A[..., j, :i]) A = (A + I) * beta[..., None, :] # (B, H, N, C, C) ``` **Phase 3: Compute w and u** ```python w = A @ (exp(g) * k) # (B, H, N, C, K) u = A @ v # (B, H, N, C, V) ``` **Phase 4: Inter-chunk recurrence** ```python S = zeros(B, H, K, V) for i in range(N): q_i = q[:, :, i] # (B, H, C, K) k_i = k[:, :, i] # (B, H, C, K) u_i = u[:, :, i] # (B, H, C, V) g_i = g[:, :, i] # (B, H, C, K) w_i = w[:, :, i] # (B, H, C, K) # Compute Aqk (lower triangular, strict upper masked) Aqk = zeros(B, H, C, C) for j in range(C): k_j = k[:, :, i, j] # (B, H, K) g_j = g[:, :, i, j:j+1] # (B, H, 1, K) Aqk[..., j] = einsum("...cd, ...d -> ...c", q_i * exp(g_i - g_j), k_j) Aqk = Aqk.masked_fill(mask_strict_upper, 0) # no diagonal v_i = u_i - w_i @ S # (B, H, C, V) o_i = (q_i * exp(g_i)) @ S + Aqk @ v_i # (B, H, C, V) # Update state S = S * exp(g_i[:, :, -1]).exp().reshape(B, H, K, 1) S = S + rearrange(exp(g_i[:,:,-1:] - g_i) * k_i, "b h c k -> b h k c") @ v_i ``` Wait, `exp(g_i[:, :, -1]).exp()` - that's `exp(exp(g_i[..., -1]))`? That seems wrong. Let me re-read. Actually, in the reference: ```python S = S * rearrange(g_i[:, :, -1].exp(), "b h k -> b h k 1") ``` This is `S = S * exp(g_i[:, :, -1]).unsqueeze(-1)`. But then the next line: ```python S = S + rearrange((g_i[:, :, -1:] - g_i).exp() * k_i, "b h c k -> b h k c") @ v_i ``` Hmm wait, the first line uses `g_i[:, :, -1].exp()` and the second uses `(g_i[:, :, -1:] - g_i).exp()`. Both are `exp(...)` not `exp(exp(...))`. But then there's `exp(g_i[:, :, -1]).exp()` which is `exp(exp(...))`. Let me look more carefully... Oh wait, I misread. The line is: ```python S = S * rearrange(g_i[:, :, -1].exp(), "b h k -> b h k 1") ``` This is `exp(g_i[:, :, -1])` reshaped. Not `exp(exp(...))`. And the next line: ```python S = S + rearrange((g_i[:, :, -1:] - g_i).exp() * k_i, "b h c k -> b h k c") @ v_i ``` This is `exp(g_i[:,:,-1:] - g_i) * k_i`, which makes sense. OK so the state update is: 1. `S = S * exp(g_i[:, :, -1]).unsqueeze(-1)` # decay the state 2. `S = S + (exp(g_i[:,:,-1:] - g_i) * k_i).transpose(-2, -1) @ v_i` # add new contributions This is a standard gated linear attention recurrence. Now, looking at FLA's code more carefully, they use `exp2` (base-2 exponential) rather than `exp` (base-e). The reference uses `.exp()` which is base-e. I need to match the reference exactly. Let me now plan my implementation strategy. ### Strategy: Triton kernels for key compute Given time constraints, I'll implement this with a combination of Triton kernels and PyTorch ops, but the key compute (A_kk build, forward substitution, w/u compute, inter-chunk) will be in Triton. Actually, let me think about what a minimal but effective implementation looks like: **Phase 1**: Cumsum on g - can be done in PyTorch or a simple Triton kernel **Phase 2**: Build A_kk. For each chunk, compute a C×C lower-triangular matrix. This is essentially: ```python for i in range(C): for j in range(i): A[..., i, j] = sum_k(k[..., i, k] * k[..., j, k] * exp(g[..., i, k] - g[..., j, k])) ``` With beta applied. This can be computed as a batched matrix multiply with masking. Actually, for a chunk of size C=64 and K=128: - For each chunk, A is 64×64 - We can compute it as: A = (k.unsqueeze(-2) * k.unsqueeze(-3) * exp(g.unsqueeze(-2) - g.unsqueeze(-3))).sum(-1) - Then apply beta and mask In PyTorch, this would be a huge memory overhead but for C=64 and K=128, it's manageable per chunk. Wait, I can do this more efficiently with einsum: ```python # k shape: (B, H, N, C, K) # g shape: (B, H, N, C, K) # For A_kk: A = torch.einsum("bhnic,bhnjc->bhnij", k * g.exp(), k * (-g).exp()) # But this computes all i,j pairs. We need exp(g_i - g_j), not exp(g_i) * exp(-g_j) # Actually: exp(g_i - g_j) = exp(g_i) * exp(-g_j), so: kg = k * g.exp() # (B, H, N, C, K) kng = k * (-g).exp() # (B, H, N, C, K) A = torch.einsum("bhnic,bhnjc->bhnij", kg, kng) ``` Wait, that's not right either. Let me think: We want `sum_k k[i,k] * k[j,k] * exp(g[i,k] - g[j,k])` = `sum_k (k[i,k] * exp(g[i,k])) * (k[j,k] * exp(-g[j,k]))` So yes: ```python kg_pos = k * g.exp() kg_neg = k * (-g).exp() A = torch.einsum("bhnic,bhnjc->bhnij", kg_pos, kg_neg) ``` But this computes the FULL matrix, including upper triangular. We need to mask it to lower triangular without diagonal. For Aqk: ```python qg_pos = q * g.exp() Aqk = torch.einsum("bhnic,bhnjc->bhnij", qg_pos, kg_neg) ``` And mask to strict lower triangular. This is much more efficient than the naive loop! And for C=64, the 64×64 matrix is tiny. Similarly, the forward substitution: ```python for i in range(1, C): A[..., i, :i] = A[..., i, :i] + (A[..., i, :, None] * A[..., :, :i]).sum(-2) ``` This is essentially: for each row i, update using all previous rows j < i. In matrix form, if we have L = A[..., :i, :i], then row i update is: `A[i, :i] = A[i, :i] + A[i, :i] @ L`... no wait. Let's expand: `sum_j A[i, j] * A[j, k]` for k < i, j can be anything < i. So for row i: `new_A[i, :i] = A[i, :i] + A[i, :i] @ A[:i, :i]` Wait, that would be `sum_j A[i,j] * A[j,k]` for j in [:i], k in [:i]. Yes! Actually, let me be more careful. The code says: ```python for i in range(1, BT): A[..., i, :i] = A[..., i, :i].clone() + (A[..., i, :, None].clone() * A[..., :, :i].clone()).sum(-2) ``` `A[..., i, :, None]` has shape (..., C, 1) `A[..., :, :i]` has shape (..., C, i) Their elementwise product has shape (..., C, i) Sum over dim -2 (the C dimension): `sum_j A[..., i, j] * A[..., j, k]` for k in [:i] But j ranges over ALL C, not just [:i]. However, A is lower triangular with zeros on and above diagonal, so A[..., i, j] = 0 for j >= i, and A[..., j, k] = 0 for j >= k (and k < i, so j >= i > k means A[j,k] could be non-zero for j < i). Wait, A is lower triangular without diagonal at this point (after `A = -A.masked_fill(mask_diag_upper, 0)`). So A[i, j] = 0 for j >= i. And for k < i, we only need j < i for A[i, j] to be non-zero. So effectively j ranges over [:i]. So: `A[i, :i] += A[i, :i] @ A[:i, :i]` This is a sequential update. For a 64×64 matrix, doing this in PyTorch per chunk is very fast. Actually, let me think about whether I can do this more efficiently in batch. The issue is that each row i depends on rows < i, so it's inherently sequential within a chunk. But across chunks and heads, it's fully parallel. For B=2, H=8, N=32 (T=2048), we have 2*8*32 = 512 independent 64×64 matrices. Doing a Python loop of 64 iterations over 512 matrices in PyTorch is trivial and very fast. So maybe the simplest approach is: 1. Use PyTorch for the small-matrix operations (A_kk build, forward substitution) 2. Use Triton for the large GEMMs and the inter-chunk recurrence Actually, let me reconsider. The main compute is in: 1. Building A_kk and Aqk: involves einsum over K dimension for each pair of positions 2. w = A @ (exp(g)*k): (B,H,N,C,C) @ (B,H,N,C,K) -> (B,H,N,C,K) 3. u = A @ v: (B,H,N,C,C) @ (B,H,N,C,V) -> (B,H,N,C,V) 4. Inter-chunk: q @ S, Aqk @ v_i, k.T @ v_i: these are K×V GEMMs For C=64, K=V=128: - A_kk build: einsum over 128 dim for 64×64 = 512K FLOPs per chunk - w: 64×64×128 = 524K FLOPs per chunk - u: 64×64×128 = 524K FLOPs per chunk - Inter-chunk per chunk: 2×64×128×128 = 2M FLOPs per chunk Total per chunk: ~3.5M FLOPs. For 512 chunks: ~1.8B FLOPs. This is actually not that much. Wait, but the problem says KDA is compute-bound. Let me recalculate. Actually for T=2048, B=2, H=8: - Total chunks = 2 * 8 * 32 = 512 - Per chunk: A_kk (64×64×128) = 524K, w (64×64×128) = 524K, u (64×64×128) = 524K, Aqk (64×64×128) = 524K, q@S (64×128×128) = 2M, Aqk@v_i (64×64×128) = 524K, state update (128×64×128) = 1M - Total per chunk ~6M FLOPs - Total = 512 * 6M = 3B FLOPs At 3B FLOPs, if we want 0.1x peak on RTX PRO 6000 (let's say ~100 TFLOPS peak for bf16), we'd need to run in 30 microseconds, which is unrealistic due to kernel launch overhead. More realistically, we might get ~1-10 TFLOPS, so 0.3-3 ms. Actually, let me check what the actual peak is for RTX PRO 6000. The problem says peak_tflops_key is bf16. Let me check the hardware module.

+"""Initial naive solution based on reference.py for baseline."""
+from __future__ import annotations
+
+import torch
+import torch.nn as nn
+from einops import rearrange
+
+
+class Model(nn.Module):
+    def __init__(self, B: int, T: int, H: int, K: int, V: int, chunk_size: int = 64):
+        super().__init__()
+        self.B, self.T, self.H, self.K, self.V = B, T, H, K, V
+        self.chunk_size = chunk_size
+        self.scale = float(K) ** -0.5
+        self.register_buffer("_dummy", torch.zeros(1), persistent=False)
+
+    def forward(
+        self,
+        q: torch.Tensor,
+        k: torch.Tensor,
+        v: torch.Tensor,
+        g: torch.Tensor,
+        beta: torch.Tensor,
+    ) -> torch.Tensor:
+        dtype = v.dtype
+        B, T, H, K = q.shape
+        V = v.shape[-1]
+        BT = self.chunk_size
+        assert T % BT == 0
+        NT = T // BT
+
+        q, k, v, g, beta = (x.to(torch.float32) for x in (q, k, v, g, beta))
+        q = q * self.scale
+
+        q = rearrange(q, "b (n c) h d -> b h n c d", c=BT)
+        k = rearrange(k, "b (n c) h d -> b h n c d", c=BT)
+        v = rearrange(v, "b (n c) h d -> b h n c d", c=BT)
+        g = rearrange(g, "b (n c) h d -> b h n c d", c=BT)
+        beta = rearrange(beta, "b (n c) h -> b h n c", c=BT)
+
+        g = g.cumsum(-2)
+
+        mask_diag_upper = torch.triu(torch.ones(BT, BT, dtype=torch.bool, device=q.device), diagonal=0)
+        A = torch.zeros(*q.shape[:-1], BT, dtype=torch.float32, device=q.device)
+        for i in range(BT):
+            k_i = k[..., i, :]
+            g_i = g[..., i:i + 1, :]
+            A[..., i] = torch.einsum("... c d, ... d -> ... c", k * (g - g_i).exp(), k_i)
+        A = A * beta[..., None]
+        A = -A.masked_fill(mask_diag_upper, 0)
+
+        for i in range(1, BT):
+            A[..., i, :i] = A[..., i, :i].clone() + (A[..., i, :, None].clone() * A[..., :, :i].clone()).sum(-2)
+        A = (A + torch.eye(BT, dtype=torch.float32, device=q.device)) * beta[..., None, :]
+
+        w = A @ (g.exp() * k)
+        u = A @ v
+
+        S = q.new_zeros(B, H, K, V)
+        o = torch.zeros_like(v)
+        mask_strict_upper = torch.triu(torch.ones(BT, BT, dtype=torch.bool, device=q.device), diagonal=1)
+        for i in range(NT):
+            q_i, k_i, u_i, g_i, w_i = q[:, :, i], k[:, :, i], u[:, :, i], g[:, :, i], w[:, :, i]
+            Aqk = torch.zeros(B, H, BT, BT, dtype=torch.float32, device=q.device)
+            for j in range(BT):
+                k_j = k[:, :, i, j]
+                g_j = g[:, :, i, j:j + 1, :]
+                Aqk[..., j] = torch.einsum("... c d, ... d -> ... c", q_i * (g_i - g_j).exp(), k_j)
+            Aqk = Aqk.masked_fill(mask_strict_upper, 0)
+            v_i = u_i - w_i @ S
+            o[:, :, i] = (q_i * g_i.exp()) @ S + Aqk @ v_i
+            S = S * rearrange(g_i[:, :, -1].exp(), "b h k -> b h k 1")
+            S = S + rearrange((g_i[:, :, -1:] - g_i).exp() * k_i, "b h c k -> b h k c") @ v_i
+
+        o = rearrange(o, "b h n c d -> b (n c) h d")
+        return o.to(dtype)

However, I can write my own kernels inspired by their approach. Actually, let me reconsider. The problem explicitly says "The intended path is CUTLASS CuTe on SM120." But writing CUTLASS kernels for this complex algorithm would take days. Let me think about what a practical Triton implementation looks like. The key insight from FLA is that they have multiple specialized kernels: 1. `chunk_kda_fwd_kernel_intra_sub_chunk` - computes diagonal blocks 2. `chunk_kda_fwd_kernel_inter_solve_fused` - computes off-diagonal + forward substitution 3. `recompute_w_u_fwd` - computes w and u 4. `chunk_gated_delta_rule_fwd_h` - inter-chunk state recurrence 5. `chunk_gla_fwd_o_gk` - final output For a much simpler but still custom approach, I could write: 1. One Triton kernel for the entire intra-chunk computation (A_kk, forward substitution, w, u, Aqk) 2. One Triton kernel for the inter-chunk recurrence But intra-chunk has a sequential forward substitution step, which is hard to parallelize within a chunk. Alternatively, I could: 1. Use PyTorch for the small sequential intra-chunk forward substitution 2. Write a Triton kernel that fuses the inter-chunk recurrence Actually, let me first see how fast I can get with a well-optimized PyTorch implementation. Let me rewrite the naive solution using vectorized ops.

import torch
import reference
import shapes

shape = shapes.SHAPES[1]
for k, v in shape.items():
    setattr(reference, k, v)

torch.manual_seed(2026)
inputs = [t.cuda() if hasattr(t, 'cuda') else t for t in reference.get_inputs()]
q, k, v, g, beta = inputs
scale = float(shape['K']) ** -0.5

B, T, H, K = q.shape
V = v.shape[-1]
BT = 64
NT = T // BT

qf = q.float() * scale
kf = k.float()
vf = v.float()
gf = g.float()
bf = beta.float()

# Reshape
qf = qf.view(B, NT, BT, H, K).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4)  # B, H, N, C, K
kf = kf.view(B, NT, BT, H, K).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4)
vf = vf.view(B, NT, BT, H, V).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4)
gf = gf.view(B, NT, BT, H, K).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4)
bf = bf.view(B, NT, BT, H).permute(0, 2, 1, 3)  # B, H, N, C

gf = gf.cumsum(-2)

# Build A_kk using einsum
kg = kf * gf.exp()
kgn = kf * (-gf).exp()

# B,H,N,C,K @ B,H,N,C,K -> B,H,N,C,C
A = torch.einsum('bhnik,bhnjk->bhnij', kg, kgn)

mask_upper = torch.triu(torch.ones(BT, BT, device=q.device, dtype=torch.bool), diagonal=0)
A = A * bf.unsqueeze(-1)
A = -A.masked_fill(mask_upper, 0)

# Forward substitution
for i in range(1, BT):
    A[:, :, :, i, :i] = A[:, :, :, i, :i] + (A[:, :, :, i, :, None] * A[:, :, :, :, :i]).sum(-2)

I = torch.eye(BT, device=q.device, dtype=torch.float32)
A = (A + I) * bf.unsqueeze(-2)

w = torch.matmul(A, (gf.exp() * kf))  # B,H,N,C,K
u = torch.matmul(A, vf)  # B,H,N,C,V

# Aqk
qg = qf * gf.exp()
Aqk = torch.einsum('bhnik,bhnjk->bhnij', qg, kgn)
mask_strict = torch.triu(torch.ones(BT, BT, device=q.device, dtype=torch.bool), diagonal=1)
Aqk = Aqk.masked_fill(mask_strict, 0)

# Inter-chunk
S = qf.new_zeros(B, H, K, V)
o = torch.zeros_like(vf)
for i in range(NT):
    q_i = qf[:, :, i]  # B,H,C,K
    k_i = kf[:, :, i]
    u_i = u[:, :, i]
    g_i = gf[:, :, i]
    w_i = w[:, :, i]
    
    v_i = u_i - torch.matmul(w_i, S)  # B,H,C,V
    qS = torch.matmul(q_i * g_i.exp(), S)  # B,H,C,V
    Av = torch.matmul(Aqk[:, :, i], v_i)  # B,H,C,V
    o[:, :, i] = qS + Av
    
    decay = g_i[:, :, -1].exp().unsqueeze(-1)  # B,H,K,1
    S = S * decay
    
    kg_decay = (g_i[:, :, -1:].unsqueeze(-2) - g_i).exp() * k_i  # B,H,C,K
    S = S + torch.matmul(kg_decay.transpose(-2, -1), v_i)

o = o.permute(0, 2, 3, 1, 4).contiguous().view(B, T, H, V)

print('Output shape:', o.shape)
print('Output dtype:', o.dtype)

torch.cuda.synchronize()
import time
start = time.time()
for _ in range(20):
    # just the inter-chunk loop
    S = qf.new_zeros(B, H, K, V)
    o = torch.zeros_like(vf)
    for i in range(NT):
        q_i = qf[:, :, i]
        k_i = kf[:, :, i]
        u_i = u[:, :, i]
        g_i = gf[:, :, i]
        w_i = w[:, :, i]
        
        v_i = u_i - torch.matmul(w_i, S)
        qS = torch.matmul(q_i * g_i.exp(), S)
        Av = torch.matmul(Aqk[:, :, i], v_i)
        o[:, :, i] = qS + Av
        
        decay = g_i[:, :, -1].exp().unsqueeze(-1)
        S = S * decay
        
        kg_decay = (g_i[:, :, -1:].unsqueeze(-2) - g_i).exp() * k_i
        S = S + torch.matmul(kg_decay.transpose(-2, -1), v_i)
torch.cuda.synchronize()
print(f'Inter-chunk only: {(time.time()-start)/20*1000:.3f} ms')

Aqk = torch.einsum('bhnik,bhnjk->bhnij', qg, kgn)
mask_strict = torch.triu(torch.ones(BT, BT, device=q.device, dtype=torch.bool), diagonal=1)

    print(f'Chunk {i}')
    q_i = qf[:, :, i]

    u_i = u[:, :, i]

    print('k_i:', k_i.shape)
    print('u_i:', u_i.shape)

    print('w_i:', w_i.shape)

    print('v_i:', v_i.shape)
    qS = torch.matmul(q_i * g_i.exp(), S)
    print('qS:', qS.shape)
    Av = torch.matmul(Aqk[:, :, i], v_i)
    print('Av:', Av.shape)
    
    decay = g_i[:, :, -1].exp().unsqueeze(-1)
    print('decay:', decay.shape)
    S = S * decay

    print('kg_decay:', kg_decay.shape)
    S = S + torch.matmul(kg_decay.transpose(-2, -1), v_i)
    print('S:', S.shape)

import torch
import reference
import shapes

shape = shapes.SHAPES[1]
for k, v in shape.items():
    setattr(reference, k, v)

torch.manual_seed(2026)
inputs = [t.cuda() if hasattr(t, 'cuda') else t for t in reference.get_inputs()]
q, k, v, g, beta = inputs
scale = float(shape['K']) ** -0.5

B, T, H, K = q.shape
V = v.shape[-1]
BT = 64
NT = T // BT

qf = q.float() * scale
kf = k.float()
vf = v.float()
gf = g.float()
bf = beta.float()

# Reshape: B,T,H,K -> B,H,N,C,K
qf = qf.view(B, NT, BT, H, K).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4)
kf = kf.view(B, NT, BT, H, K).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4)
vf = vf.view(B, NT, BT, H, V).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4)
gf = gf.view(B, NT, BT, H, K).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4)
bf = bf.view(B, NT, BT, H).permute(0, 3, 1, 2)  # B,H,N,C

gf = gf.cumsum(-2)

kg = kf * gf.exp()
kgn = kf * (-gf).exp()
A = torch.einsum('bhnik,bhnjk->bhnij', kg, kgn)
mask_upper = torch.triu(torch.ones(BT, BT, device=q.device, dtype=torch.bool), diagonal=0)
A = A * bf[..., None]
A = -A.masked_fill(mask_upper, 0)
for i in range(1, BT):
    A[..., i, :i] = A[..., i, :i] + (A[..., i, :, None] * A[..., :, :i]).sum(-2)
I = torch.eye(BT, device=q.device, dtype=torch.float32)
A = (A + I) * bf[..., None, :]

w = torch.matmul(A, (gf.exp() * kf))
u = torch.matmul(A, vf)

qg = qf * gf.exp()
Aqk = torch.einsum('bhnik,bhnjk->bhnij', qg, kgn)
mask_strict = torch.triu(torch.ones(BT, BT, device=q.device, dtype=torch.bool), diagonal=1)
Aqk = Aqk.masked_fill(mask_strict, 0)

S = qf.new_zeros(B, H, K, V)
o = torch.zeros_like(vf)
for i in range(NT):
    q_i = qf[:, :, i]
    k_i = kf[:, :, i]
    u_i = u[:, :, i]
    g_i = gf[:, :, i]
    w_i = w[:, :, i]
    
    v_i = u_i - torch.matmul(w_i, S)
    qS = torch.matmul(q_i * g_i.exp(), S)
    Av = torch.matmul(Aqk[:, :, i], v_i)
    o[:, :, i] = qS + Av
    
    decay = g_i[:, :, -1].exp().unsqueeze(-1)  # B,H,K,1
    S = S * decay
    
    # g_i[:, :, -1:] is (B,H,1,128), g_i is (B,H,C,128)
    kg_decay = (g_i[:, :, -1:] - g_i).exp() * k_i  # B,H,C,K
    S = S + torch.matmul(kg_decay.transpose(-2, -1), v_i)

o = o.permute(0, 2, 3, 1, 4).contiguous().view(B, T, H, V)

# Check correctness
ref_model = reference.Model(B, T, H, K, V, BT).cuda()
with torch.no_grad():
    ref_out = ref_model(*inputs)

We need at least 10% to pass the benchmark's OK threshold. So I need to be about 18x faster. The main bottleneck in my vectorized version is probably the inter-chunk loop, which has many small kernel launches. Each iteration of the NT=32 loop launches several small GEMMs, and there's significant kernel launch overhead. To get to SOTA performance, I need to fuse the inter-chunk recurrence into a single kernel, similar to what FLA does. Let me think about what's achievable with Triton. I can write: 1. A Triton kernel for the intra-chunk A_kk computation and forward substitution 2. A Triton kernel for the inter-chunk recurrence that processes chunks sequentially within each thread block but parallelizes across B*H For the inter-chunk kernel, each thread block could handle one (B, H) pair and iterate through chunks sequentially. Within each chunk, it would: 1. Load q_i, k_i, v_i (actually u_i, w_i, Aqk are precomputed) 2. Compute v_i = u_i - w_i @ S 3. Compute o_i = (q_i * exp(g_i)) @ S + Aqk @ v_i 4. Update S The challenge is that w_i @ S is (C,K) @ (K,V), which for C=64, K=128, V=128 is a small matmul. Similarly for the others. In Triton, I can tile these operations. For example, with tile sizes of 32 or 64. Actually, let me think about this more carefully. The inter-chunk recurrence in FLA is done by `chunk_gated_delta_rule_fwd_h` and `chunk_gla_fwd_o_gk`. reasoning (3266 chars)
This FLA kernel is very sophisticated. It tiles the state S into 64×BV blocks and processes chunks sequentially within each thread block. The key insight is: - Each thread block handles one (batch, head) and one tile of V dimension - K is always handled in 64-wide tiles (since K=128, it's 2 tiles) - V is tiled by BV (32 or 64) For my implementation, I can take a simpler approach. Since K=V=128, and chunk_size=64, I can write a Triton kernel that: 1. Each thread block handles one (B, H) pair 2. Loads the entire K×V state (128×128 = 16K floats = 64KB) into shared memory 3. Iterates through chunks sequentially 4. Within each chunk, computes the required matmuls But 64KB of shared memory for the state is a lot. On SM120, shared memory is probably 228KB per SM, so 64KB is feasible but might limit occupancy. Alternatively, I can tile V into smaller chunks and have multiple thread blocks per (B, H) pair, similar to FLA. Actually, for a simpler kernel, I can just have each thread block handle one (B, H) and process all V channels. With K=V=128, the state is 128×128 floats = 64KB. If I use fp32 accumulators, that's fine. For the intermediate computations (v_i, o_i), I need C×V = 64×128 = 8K floats = 32KB. Total shared memory: 64KB (state) + 32KB (v_i) + some for loading q, k, w, etc. = ~100-120KB. This is reasonable. Actually, in Triton we don't explicitly manage shared memory - the compiler does it for us. But we can reason about register pressure. For K=128, V=128, C=64: - State S: 128×128 fp32 = 16K elements - Loading q_i: 64×128 = 8K elements - Loading k_i: 64×128 = 8K elements - Loading w_i: 64×128 = 8K elements - Loading u_i: 64×128 = 8K elements - Loading Aqk: 64×64 = 4K elements - v_i: 64×128 = 8K elements - o_i: 64×128 = 8K elements If each thread block has 128 threads (4 warps), each thread would need to hold ~500 fp32 values in registers if we loaded everything. That's too much. So we need to tile. Let's tile V into 64-wide chunks. Then: - State tile: 128×64 = 8K fp32 - v_i tile: 64×64 = 4K fp32 - o_i tile: 64×64 = 4K fp32 And we process K in 64-wide chunks too for the matmuls. This is similar to FLA's approach but simpler. Let me think about the kernel design: **Grid**: (V_tiles, B*H) where V_tiles = V // BV = 128 // 64 = 2 **Tile sizes**: BV=64 for V, BK=64 for K For each thread block (i_v, i_bh): - i_v = tile index in V dimension (0 or 1) - i_bh = batch*head index State S_tile = zeros(128, 64) fp32 For each chunk i in 0..NT-1: 1. Load u_i[:, i_v*BV:(i_v+1)*BV] -> (64, 64) 2. Load w_i in K tiles of 64: w_i1, w_i2 -> (64, 64) each 3. Compute v_i = u_i - w_i @ S_tile - w_i1 (64,64) @ S_tile1 (64,64) -> partial - w_i2 (64,64) @ S_tile2 (64,64) -> partial 4. Load q_i in K tiles, g_i in K tiles 5. Compute qS = (q_i * exp(g_i)) @ S_tile 6. Load Aqk (64,64) 7. Compute Aqk @ v_i 8. o_i = qS + Aqk @ v_i 9. Store o_i 10. Update S: - decay = exp(g_i[:, -1]) # (128,) - S_tile *= decay[:, None] - Load k_i in K tiles - Compute kg_decay = exp(g_i[-1:] - g_i) * k_i # (64, 128) - S_tile += kg_decay.T @ v_i This is getting complex. Let me look at how FLA's `chunk_gla_fwd_o_gk` works since it's similar to what I need for the output computation.

import torch
import reference
import shapes

shape = shapes.SHAPES[1]

    setattr(reference, k, v)

inputs = [t.cuda() if hasattr(t, 'cuda') else t for t in reference.get_inputs()]
q, k, v, g, beta = inputs
scale = float(shape['K']) ** -0.5

B, T, H, K = q.shape

kf = k.float()
vf = v.float()

bf = beta.float()

qf = qf.view(B, NT, BT, H, K).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4)
kf = kf.view(B, NT, BT, H, K).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4)
vf = vf.view(B, NT, BT, H, V).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4)
gf = gf.view(B, NT, BT, H, K).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4)
bf = bf.view(B, NT, BT, H).permute(0, 3, 1, 2)

mask_upper = torch.triu(torch.ones(BT, BT, device=q.device, dtype=torch.bool), diagonal=0)
mask_strict = torch.triu(torch.ones(BT, BT, device=q.device, dtype=torch.bool), diagonal=1)
I = torch.eye(BT, device=q.device, dtype=torch.float32)

gf = gf.cumsum(-2)

torch.cuda.synchronize()
import time
start = time.time()
for _ in range(100):
    kg = kf * gf.exp()
    kgn = kf * (-gf).exp()
    A = torch.einsum('bhnik,bhnjk->bhnij', kg, kgn)
    A = A * bf[..., None]
    A = -A.masked_fill(mask_upper, 0)
    for i in range(1, BT):
        A[..., i, :i] = A[..., i, :i] + (A[..., i, :, None] * A[..., :, :i]).sum(-2)
    A = (A + I) * bf[..., None, :]
    w = torch.matmul(A, (gf.exp() * kf))
    u = torch.matmul(A, vf)
    qg = qf * gf.exp()
    Aqk = torch.einsum('bhnik,bhnjk->bhnij', qg, kgn)
    Aqk = Aqk.masked_fill(mask_strict, 0)
torch.cuda.synchronize()
print(f'Intra-chunk: {(time.time()-start)/100*1000:.3f} ms')

kgn = kf * (-gf).exp()
A = torch.einsum('bhnik,bhnjk->bhnij', kg, kgn)
A = A * bf[..., None]
A = -A.masked_fill(mask_upper, 0)

u = torch.matmul(A, vf)

Aqk = Aqk.masked_fill(mask_strict, 0)

for _ in range(100):
    S = qf.new_zeros(B, H, K, V)
    out = torch.zeros_like(vf)
    for i in range(NT):
        q_i = qf[:, :, i]
        k_i = kf[:, :, i]
        u_i = u[:, :, i]
        g_i = gf[:, :, i]
        w_i = w[:, :, i]
        
        v_i = u_i - torch.matmul(w_i, S)
        qS = torch.matmul(q_i * g_i.exp(), S)
        Av = torch.matmul(Aqk[:, :, i], v_i)
        out[:, :, i] = qS + Av
        
        decay = g_i[:, :, -1].exp().unsqueeze(-1)
        S = S * decay
        
        kg_decay = (g_i[:, :, -1:] - g_i).exp() * k_i
        S = S + torch.matmul(kg_decay.transpose(-2, -1), v_i)
torch.cuda.synchronize()
print(f'Inter-chunk: {(time.time()-start)/100*1000:.3f} ms')

Interesting! einsum is actually faster than bmm in this case. Probably because einsum can use a more optimized contraction path. Let me try to optimize the forward substitution loop, which might be a significant bottleneck. For the forward substitution, I'm doing: ```python for i in range(1, BT): A[..., i, :i] = A[..., i, :i] + (A[..., i, :, None] * A[..., :, :i]).sum(-2) ``` This involves a Python loop over 64 iterations. Each iteration does batched ops on tensors of shape (B*H*N, 1, i) and (B*H*N, C, i). For large i, this is a decent-sized matmul. But for small i, it's very small. Actually, I can vectorize this further. The operation is: For each matrix A of shape (C, C), we want to compute: for i in range(1, C): A[i, :i] = A[i, :i] + A[i, :] @ A[:, :i] But A[i, j] = 0 for j >= i, so A[i, :] @ A[:, :i] = A[i, :i] @ A[:i, :i] So the update is: A[i, :i] += A[i, :i] @ A[:i, :i] This is still sequential. But we can use the fact that this is essentially computing a matrix power series. Actually, this is computing (I + A)^{-1} through forward substitution. For a 64×64 matrix, a custom CUDA kernel would be ideal, but in PyTorch we might just accept the loop. Wait, there's actually a more efficient way. This is computing the inverse of (I - L) where L is strictly lower triangular. The inverse is I + L + L^2 + L^3 + ... + L^{C-1}. We can compute this by repeated squaring! Actually, (I - L)^{-1} = I + L + L^2 + ... . Since L is nilpotent (L^C = 0), we can compute it as: S = I for _ in range(log2(C)): S = S + S @ (L @ S) # or something similar Wait, that's not quite right either. Let me think... For forward substitution on (I - L)x = b, the solution is x = (I + L + L^2 + ...)b. The matrix inverse is (I - L)^{-1} = sum_{k=0}^{C-1} L^k. We can compute this by binary splitting: - Compute L^2, L^4, L^8, ... by repeated squaring - Then the sum is I + L + L^2 + L^3 + ... Actually, there's a well-known parallel algorithm for this. We can compute the prefix products. But in PyTorch, for C=64, the loop might just be fine. Let me profile the forward substitution to see how much time it takes.

for _ in range(1000):
    A_copy = A.clone()
    for i in range(1, C):

reasoning (9006 chars)
The forward substitution loop takes 1.2ms, which is a huge bottleneck. A single BMM of the same size takes only 0.049ms, so the loop is ~25x slower than it should be. This is because the loop has 64 iterations, and each iteration launches a small kernel. The kernel launch overhead dominates. To fix this, I need to either: 1. Write a custom CUDA kernel for the forward substitution 2. Find a way to parallelize it 3. Use a different algorithm For option 2, there is actually a parallel prefix-based algorithm for matrix inversion of triangular matrices. We can use the "parallel cyclic reduction" or "divide and conquer" approach. For a lower triangular matrix L, the forward substitution for (I - L)^{-1} can be computed recursively: - Split L into blocks: L = [L11, 0; L21, L22] - Then (I - L)^{-1} = [(I-L11)^{-1}, 0; (I-L22)^{-1} @ L21 @ (I-L11)^{-1}, (I-L22)^{-1}] This is a divide-and-conquer approach that can be parallelized! For C=64, we can do: - Level 0: Split into 2×2 blocks of size 32 - Level 1: Each 32 block splits into 2×2 blocks of size 16 - etc. At the leaf level (size 1), the inverse is trivial (just 1). Then we merge level by level. This is much more parallel and can be done with batched matrix multiplications! Let me write this out for C=64: - Level 0 (leaf): blocks of size 1. There are 64 diagonal blocks, each is [1]. The strictly lower blocks are just 0. - Level 1: merge pairs of size-1 blocks into size-2 blocks. For a size-2 block [1, 0; l21, 1], the inverse is [1, 0; -l21, 1]. Wait, but in our case, the matrix is (I + A) where A is strictly lower triangular. Actually, we want to compute (I + A)^{-1} where A is the lower-triangular matrix after applying -A.masked_fill and the forward substitution. Wait, let me re-read the reference more carefully. In the reference: 1. A = einsum(k * exp(g - g_i), k_i) * beta 2. A = -A.masked_fill(upper, 0) # strictly lower triangular, negated 3. Forward substitution: for i: A[i, :i] += A[i, :] @ A[:, :i] 4. A = (A + I) * beta[..., None, :] So after step 3, A is (I - L)^{-1} for some strictly lower triangular L. Then step 4 multiplies by beta on the right. Actually wait. After step 2, A is -L where L is strictly lower triangular with the original values. Step 3 computes the forward substitution on -L. If we define M = I - (-L) = I + L, then step 3 computes M^{-1}. Wait no... Let's trace carefully. After step 2: A[i, j] = -L[i, j] for i > j, where L[i, j] = einsum(k_j * exp(g_j - g_i), k_i) * beta_i Wait, in the reference: ```python A[..., i] = torch.einsum("... c d, ... d -> ... c", k * (g - g_i).exp(), k_i) A = A * beta[..., None] A = -A.masked_fill(mask_diag_upper, 0) ``` So A[i, j] = -beta_i * sum_d k[j,d] * k[i,d] * exp(g[j,d] - g[i,d]) for j < i. Then the forward substitution: ```python for i in range(1, BT): A[..., i, :i] = A[..., i, :i] + (A[..., i, :, None] * A[..., :, :i]).sum(-2) ``` This is: new_A[i, j] = A[i, j] + sum_k A[i, k] * A[k, j] for j < i, k < i. Since A[i, k] = 0 for k >= i, and A[k, j] = 0 for k <= j, the sum is over j < k < i. This is computing: A = A + A^2 + A^3 + ... = A * (I + A + A^2 + ...) = A * (I - A)^{-1} Wait, if A is strictly lower triangular, then I - A is invertible, and (I - A)^{-1} = I + A + A^2 + A^3 + ... So after the loop, we have: A_final = A + A^2 + A^3 + ... = A * (I - A)^{-1} = (I - A)^{-1} - I Then: A = (A_final + I) * beta = (I - A)^{-1} * beta Ah! So the forward substitution computes (I - A)^{-1} where A is the original strictly lower triangular matrix (before the loop). And then we multiply by beta. So if I can compute (I - A)^{-1} efficiently, that's the key. For a strictly lower triangular matrix, there is indeed a divide-and-conquer approach: Given A of size C×C, strictly lower triangular. Split into 4 blocks of size C/2: A = [A11, 0; A21, A22] Then I - A = [I - A11, 0; -A21, I - A22] The inverse is: (I - A)^{-1} = [(I-A11)^{-1}, 0; (I-A22)^{-1} @ A21 @ (I-A11)^{-1}, (I-A22)^{-1}] This can be computed recursively! For C=64: - Base case: C=1, (I - A)^{-1} = [1] - Level 1: C=2, compute inverses of two 1×1 blocks, then the off-diagonal - Level 2: C=4, compute inverses of two 2×2 blocks, then the off-diagonal - ... - Level 6: C=64 At each level, for B*H*N = 512 matrices, we do: 1. Two inverses of size C/2 (computed recursively in parallel) 2. One matmul: (C/2, C/2) @ (C/2, C/2) @ (C/2, C/2) for the off-diagonal For C=64, at each level the total matmul size is 512 × (C/2)^3 FLOPs. Summing over levels: - Level 1 (C=2): 512 × 1^3 = 512 FLOPs - Level 2 (C=4): 512 × 2^3 = 4096 FLOPs - Level 3 (C=8): 512 × 4^3 = 32768 FLOPs - Level 4 (C=16): 512 × 8^3 = 262K FLOPs - Level 5 (C=32): 512 × 16^3 = 2M FLOPs - Level 6 (C=64): 512 × 32^3 = 16.7M FLOPs Total: ~19.3M FLOPs. At 200 TFLOPS, this is 0.0001 ms. Of course, the actual time is dominated by kernel launch overhead and memory movement, not compute. But even if each level takes 0.01ms, total is 0.06ms, which is 20x faster than the 1.2ms loop! Let me implement this divide-and-conquer approach in PyTorch. Actually, I realize there's an even simpler way. Since C=64 is a power of 2, I can implement the divide-and-conquer iteratively: ```python def tri_inverse(A): """A is (..., C, C) strictly lower triangular. Returns (I - A)^{-1}.""" C = A.shape[-1] result = torch.eye(C, device=A.device, dtype=A.dtype).expand_as(A).clone() # Work with block sizes: 1, 2, 4, 8, 16, 32 for block_size in [1, 2, 4, 8, 16, 32]: num_blocks = C // block_size # For each pair of diagonal blocks, compute the off-diagonal for i in range(0, num_blocks, 2): if i + 1 < num_blocks: # Top-left block of the pair: result[i*bs:(i+1)*bs, i*bs:(i+1)*bs] # Bottom-right block: result[(i+1)*bs:(i+2)*bs, (i+1)*bs:(i+2)*bs] # Off-diagonal A block: A[(i+1)*bs:(i+2)*bs, i*bs:(i+1)*bs] # New off-diagonal = bottom_right @ A_off @ top_left top = result[..., i*block_size:(i+1)*block_size, i*block_size:(i+1)*block_size] bot = result[..., (i+1)*block_size:(i+2)*block_size, (i+1)*block_size:(i+2)*block_size] off = A[..., (i+1)*block_size:(i+2)*block_size, i*block_size:(i+1)*block_size] result[..., (i+1)*block_size:(i+2)*block_size, i*block_size:(i+1)*block_size] = \ torch.matmul(torch.matmul(bot, off), top) return result ``` Wait, this is wrong. The divide-and-conquer needs to be bottom-up, not top-down. Let me think more carefully. At the base case, each diagonal block is just I. Then we merge adjacent blocks. For merging two adjacent blocks of size s: - Top-left is T (s×s) - Bottom-right is B (s×s) - Off-diagonal in A is O (s×s) - The merged block's inverse has off-diagonal = B @ O @ T But T and B themselves are inverses of (I - A_top) and (I - A_bot), which we computed at the previous level. So the algorithm is: 1. Initialize result = I 2. For level in 0, 1, 2, 3, 4, 5 (block sizes 1, 2, 4, 8, 16, 32): - For each pair of blocks at this level: - off = A[..., bottom_block, top_block] - top = result[..., top_block, top_block] - bot = result[..., bottom_block, bottom_block] - result[..., bottom_block, top_block] = bot @ off @ top This is correct! Let me verify. For C=4: - Start: result = I (4×4) - Level 0 (block_size=1): - Pair (0,1): off=A[1,0], top=I[0,0]=1, bot=I[1,1]=1. result[1,0] = 1*A[1,0]*1 = A[1,0] - Pair (2,3): off=A[3,2], top=I[2,2]=1, bot=I[3,3]=1. result[3,2] = A[3,2] - Level 1 (block_size=2): - Pair (0,1): blocks [0:2] and [2:4] - off=A[2:4, 0:2] - top=result[0:2, 0:2] - bot=result[2:4, 2:4] - result[2:4, 0:2] = bot @ off @ top At level 0, top and bot are I, so result[0:2, 0:2] = (I - A[0:2, 0:2])^{-1} and result[2:4, 2:4] = (I - A[2:4, 2:4])^{-1}. At level 1, we compute the off-diagonal using the formula. This gives us the full inverse of the 4×4 block. Yes! This works. And each level can be parallelized as a single batched matmul over all pairs. For C=64, there are 6 levels. At level k (block_size = 2^k), there are 64 / 2^(k+1) pairs. Each pair requires a matmul of size (2^k, 2^k) @ (2^k, 2^k) @ (2^k, 2^k). For B*H*N = 512 matrices: - Level 0 (bs=1): 32 pairs, each matmul is (512, 1, 1) @ (512, 1, 1) = trivial - Level 1 (bs=2): 16 pairs, each matmul is (512, 2, 2) @ (512, 2, 2) - Level 2 (bs=4): 8 pairs, matmul (512, 4, 4) @ (512, 4, 4) - Level 3 (bs=8): 4 pairs, matmul (512, 8, 8) @ (512, 8, 8) - Level 4 (bs=16): 2 pairs, matmul (512, 16, 16) @ (512, 16, 16) - Level 5 (bs=32): 1 pair, matmul (512, 32, 32) @ (512, 32, 32) We can batch all pairs within a level together into a single bmm. For example, at level 2: - Stack all 8 pairs into a batch of 512*8 = 4096 matrices of size 4×4 - Do bmm in one call Actually, even better: for each level, we can reshape the tensors to do all pairs in parallel. Let me implement and test this.

import torch
import time

def tri_inv_dc(A):
    '''A is (..., C, C) strictly lower triangular. Returns (I - A)^{-1}.'''
    C = A.shape[-1]
    result = torch.eye(C, device=A.device, dtype=A.dtype).expand_as(A).clone()
    
    bs = 1
    while bs < C:
        num_pairs = C // (2 * bs)
        for p in range(num_pairs):
            top_start = p * 2 * bs
            top_end = top_start + bs
            bot_start = top_end
            bot_end = bot_start + bs
            
            top = result[..., top_start:top_end, top_start:top_end]
            bot = result[..., bot_start:bot_end, bot_start:bot_end]
            off = A[..., bot_start:bot_end, top_start:top_end]
            
            result[..., bot_start:bot_end, top_start:top_end] = torch.matmul(torch.matmul(bot, off), top)
        bs *= 2
    
    return result

# Test correctness
C = 64
BHN = 512
A = torch.randn(BHN, C, C, device='cuda') * 0.1
mask = torch.tril(torch.ones(C, C, device='cuda'), diagonal=-1)
A = A * mask

# Reference: sequential forward substitution
ref = torch.eye(C, device='cuda').expand_as(A).clone()
ref[:, 0, 0] = 1
for i in range(1, C):
    ref[:, i, :i] = A[:, i, :i] + (A[:, i, :, None] * ref[:, :, :i]).sum(-2)
    ref[:, i, i] = 1

# DC approach
dc = tri_inv_dc(A)

print('Max diff:', (ref - dc).abs().max().item())

# Time
torch.cuda.synchronize()
start = time.time()
for _ in range(1000):
    _ = tri_inv_dc(A)
torch.cuda.synchronize()
print(f'DC time: {(time.time()-start)/1000*1000:.3f} ms')

        ref[:, i, i] = 1
torch.cuda.synchronize()
print(f'Loop time: {(time.time()-start)/1000*1000:.3f} ms')

Now let me verify my DC algorithm. The formula for the inverse of a block triangular matrix: If M = [A, 0; C, B], then M^{-1} = [A^{-1}, 0; -B^{-1} @ C @ A^{-1}, B^{-1}]. import torch

def reference_fwd_subst(A):
    C = A.shape[-1]
    for i in range(1, C):
        A[..., i, :i] = A[..., i, :i].clone() + (A[..., i, :, None].clone() * A[..., :, :i].clone()).sum(-2)
    return A

def tri_inv_dc(A):
    C = A.shape[-1]
    result = torch.eye(C, device=A.device, dtype=A.dtype).expand_as(A).clone()
    
    bs = 1
    while bs < C:
        num_pairs = C // (2 * bs)
        for p in range(num_pairs):
            top_start = p * 2 * bs
            top_end = top_start + bs
            bot_start = top_end
            bot_end = bot_start + bs
            
            top = result[..., top_start:top_end, top_start:top_end]
            bot = result[..., bot_start:bot_end, bot_start:bot_end]
            off = A[..., bot_start:bot_end, top_start:top_end]
            
            result[..., bot_start:bot_end, top_start:top_end] = torch.matmul(torch.matmul(bot, off), top)
        bs *= 2
    
    return result

# Test with small C
for C in [2, 4, 8, 16, 32, 64]:
    A = torch.randn(4, C, C, device='cuda') * 0.1
    mask = torch.tril(torch.ones(C, C, device='cuda'), diagonal=-1)
    A = A * mask
    
    ref = reference_fwd_subst(A.clone()) + torch.eye(C, device='cuda')
    dc = tri_inv_dc(A)
    
    print(f'C={C}: max_diff={(ref - dc).abs().max().item():.6f}')

import torch
import time

def tri_inv_dc(A):
    C = A.shape[-1]
    result = torch.eye(C, device=A.device, dtype=A.dtype).expand_as(A).clone()
    
    bs = 1
    while bs < C:
        num_pairs = C // (2 * bs)
        # Batch all pairs into a single bmm
        top_list = []
        bot_list = []
        off_list = []
        for p in range(num_pairs):
            top_start = p * 2 * bs
            top_end = top_start + bs
            bot_start = top_end
            bot_end = bot_start + bs
            top_list.append(result[..., top_start:top_end, top_start:top_end])
            bot_list.append(result[..., bot_start:bot_end, bot_start:bot_end])
            off_list.append(A[..., bot_start:bot_end, top_start:top_end])
        
        if num_pairs > 0:
            # Stack and bmm
            tops = torch.stack(top_list, dim=-4)  # (num_pairs, ..., bs, bs)
            bots = torch.stack(bot_list, dim=-4)
            offs = torch.stack(off_list, dim=-4)
            
            # Reshape to merge batch dims
            batch_shape = A.shape[:-2]
            tops_flat = tops.view(num_pairs, -1, bs, bs)
            bots_flat = bots.view(num_pairs, -1, bs, bs)
            offs_flat = offs.view(num_pairs, -1, bs, bs)
            
            # bot @ off
            tmp = torch.bmm(bots_flat, offs_flat)
            # tmp @ top
            out = torch.bmm(tmp, tops_flat)
            
            # Unstack back
            out = out.view(num_pairs, *batch_shape, bs, bs)
            for p in range(num_pairs):
                top_start = p * 2 * bs
                top_end = top_start + bs
                bot_start = top_end
                bot_end = bot_start + bs
                result[..., bot_start:bot_end, top_start:top_end] = out[p]
        
        bs *= 2
    
    return result

# Performance test
C = 64
BHN = 512
A = torch.randn(BHN, C, C, device='cuda') * 0.1
mask = torch.tril(torch.ones(C, C, device='cuda'), diagonal=-1)
A = A * mask

import torch
import time

def tri_inv_dc(A):
    C = A.shape[-1]
    result = torch.eye(C, device=A.device, dtype=A.dtype).expand_as(A).clone()
    batch_shape = A.shape[:-2]
    batch_numel = batch_shape.numel()
    
    bs = 1
    while bs < C:
        num_pairs = C // (2 * bs)
        if num_pairs > 0:
            # Stack all pairs
            tops = []
            bots = []
            offs = []
            for p in range(num_pairs):
                top_start = p * 2 * bs
                top_end = top_start + bs
                bot_start = top_end
                bot_end = bot_start + bs
                tops.append(result[..., top_start:top_end, top_start:top_end])
                bots.append(result[..., bot_start:bot_end, bot_start:bot_end])
                offs.append(A[..., bot_start:bot_end, top_start:top_end])
            
            # Shape: (num_pairs, batch_numel, bs, bs)
            tops_t = torch.stack(tops, dim=0).view(num_pairs * batch_numel, bs, bs)
            bots_t = torch.stack(bots, dim=0).view(num_pairs * batch_numel, bs, bs)
            offs_t = torch.stack(offs, dim=0).view(num_pairs * batch_numel, bs, bs)
            
            tmp = torch.bmm(bots_t, offs_t)
            out = torch.bmm(tmp, tops_t)
            out = out.view(num_pairs, *batch_shape, bs, bs)
            
            for p in range(num_pairs):
                top_start = p * 2 * bs
                top_end = top_start + bs
                bot_start = top_end
                bot_end = bot_start + bs
                result[..., bot_start:bot_end, top_start:top_end] = out[p]
        
        bs *= 2
    
    return result

# Performance test
C = 64
BHN = 512
A = torch.randn(BHN, C, C, device='cuda') * 0.1
mask = torch.tril(torch.ones(C, C, device='cuda'), diagonal=-1)
A = A * mask

torch.cuda.synchronize()
start = time.time()
for _ in range(1000):
    _ = tri_inv_dc(A)
torch.cuda.synchronize()
print(f'Batched DC: {(time.time()-start)/1000*1000:.3f} ms')

# Reference
torch.cuda.synchronize()
start = time.time()
for _ in range(1000):
    ref = A.clone()
    for i in range(1, C):
        ref[:, i, :i] = ref[:, i, :i] + (ref[:, i, :, None] * ref[:, :, :i]).sum(-2)
torch.cuda.synchronize()
print(f'Loop: {(time.time()-start)/1000*1000:.3f} ms')

reasoning (4118 chars)
The batched DC approach is 0.874ms vs 1.221ms for the loop - only about 1.4x faster, not the dramatic improvement I was hoping for. The issue is that even with batched bmm, the matrices are very small (up to 32×32), and bmm has significant overhead for small matrices. For C=64, at bs=32, we're doing a bmm of (512, 32, 32) @ (512, 32, 32) @ (512, 32, 32). Actually we're doing two bmms: first (512, 32, 32) @ (512, 32, 32), then the result @ (512, 32, 32). A 32×32 bmm for batch 512 is still quite small. Let me try a different approach: write a custom CUDA kernel for the forward substitution using `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline`. For a 64×64 strictly lower triangular matrix, a single thread block can handle one matrix, with each thread handling one element. Actually, for 512 matrices and 64×64=4096 elements each, we could use 512 thread blocks with 64 threads each, where each thread computes one row. The row computation is sequential, but with only 64 steps, and 512 thread blocks, this would be very fast. Let me think about writing a simple CUDA kernel: ```cuda __global__ void forward_subst_kernel(float* A, int C, int batch) { int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; if (b >= batch) return; float* mat = A + b * C * C; // Each thread handles one row i for (int i = tid + 1; i < C; i += blockDim.x) { for (int j = 0; j < i; j++) { float sum = 0; for (int k = 0; k < i; k++) { sum += mat[i * C + k] * mat[k * C + j]; } mat[i * C + j] += sum; } } } ``` Wait, but in the reference, the sum uses the IN-PLACE updated values. For row i, when computing the sum over k, A[i, k] uses the ORIGINAL value (since row i hasn't been updated yet), but A[k, j] uses the UPDATED value if k < i. So for a thread computing row i: ```python for j in range(i): sum = 0 for k in range(i): sum += orig_A[i, k] * updated_A[k, j] new_A[i, j] = orig_A[i, j] + sum ``` But `orig_A[i, k]` is just the current value before updating row i. And `updated_A[k, j]` is the current value of the matrix at [k, j]. So each thread for row i can read the entire original row i once, then compute the updated row i using the current matrix values. Actually, there's a race condition if multiple threads in the same block try to update different rows simultaneously. Thread for row i needs to read updated_A[k, j] for k < i, which might be written by other threads. We can synchronize between rows using __syncthreads(), but that means we process rows sequentially within a block, which defeats the purpose of multiple threads. Better approach: one thread block per matrix, but only one thread active (or all threads computing the same row in parallel, then syncing). Actually, for a 64×64 matrix, we can have one warp (32 threads) compute each row. Two warps = 64 threads. For each row i: 1. Load orig_A[i, 0:i] into registers 2. Compute updated_A[i, j] for j in [0, i) using parallel reduction across threads - Each thread computes a partial sum over k - Then accumulate Wait, the operation is: new_A[i, j] = orig_A[i, j] + sum_k orig_A[i, k] * A[k, j] For fixed i and j, this is a dot product of orig_A[i, 0:i] and A[0:i, j]. We can compute all j simultaneously using matrix-vector multiplication: new_A[i, 0:i] = orig_A[i, 0:i] + orig_A[i, 0:i] @ A[0:i, 0:i] This is a vector-matrix product! For each row i, we do: 1. Load v = orig_A[i, 0:i] 2. Compute v @ A[0:i, 0:i] 3. new_A[i, 0:i] = orig_A[i, 0:i] + result This can be done as a matmul of (1, i) @ (i, i) -> (1, i). For i=64, this is a 1×64 @ 64×64 matmul. Still tiny. Hmm, maybe the simplest approach is to just use a single thread per matrix and do the loop. With 512 matrices and 512 thread blocks, each doing a 64-iteration loop, this should be very fast. A single thread can do ~10 GFLOPS in fp32, so 512 threads doing ~64^3/3 ≈ 87K FLOPs each = 45M FLOPs total. At 10 GFLOPS per thread, that's 0.0045 ms. With memory bandwidth, maybe 0.05ms. That's much better than 0.87ms! Let me write this CUDA kernel.

import torch
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

cuda_source = '''
__global__ void fwd_subst_kernel(float* __restrict__ A, int C, int batch) {
    int b = blockIdx.x;
    if (b >= batch) return;
    
    float* mat = A + b * C * C;
    
    for (int i = 1; i < C; i++) {
        for (int j = 0; j < i; j++) {
            float sum = 0.0f;
            for (int k = 0; k < i; k++) {
                sum += mat[i * C + k] * mat[k * C + j];
            }
            mat[i * C + j] += sum;
        }
    }
}

torch::Tensor fwd_subst(torch::Tensor A) {
    int batch = A.size(0);
    int C = A.size(1);
    fwd_subst_kernel<<<batch, 1>>>(A.data_ptr<float>(), C, batch);
    return A;
}
'''

module = load_inline(
    name='fwd_subst',
    cpp_sources='torch::Tensor fwd_subst(torch::Tensor A);',
    cuda_sources=cuda_source,
    functions=['fwd_subst'],
    extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3'],
    verbose=False,
)

C = 64
BHN = 512
A = torch.randn(BHN, C, C, device='cuda') * 0.1
mask = torch.tril(torch.ones(C, C, device='cuda'), diagonal=-1)
A = (A * mask).contiguous()

# Correctness
ref = A.clone()
for i in range(1, C):
    ref[:, i, :i] = ref[:, i, :i] + (ref[:, i, :, None] * ref[:, :, :i]).sum(-2)

out = module.fwd_subst(A.clone())
print('Max diff:', (ref - out).abs().max().item())

# Timing
torch.cuda.synchronize()
import time
start = time.time()
for _ in range(10000):
    _ = module.fwd_subst(A.clone())
torch.cuda.synchronize()
print(f'CUDA kernel: {(time.time()-start)/10000*1000:.3f} ms')

The single-thread-per-matrix CUDA kernel is 0.979ms, which is actually slightly slower than the batched DC approach (0.874ms). This is because a single thread per block is very inefficient on the GPU - most of the warp is idle. Let me try a better CUDA kernel where each block handles one matrix but all 64 threads cooperate. We can process rows sequentially but compute each row's update in parallel across threads. For row i, we need to compute: new_A[i, j] = orig_A[i, j] + sum_{k=0}^{i-1} orig_A[i, k] * A[k, j] for j in [0, i) This is: new_A[i, 0:i] = orig_A[i, 0:i] + orig_A[i, 0:i] @ A[0:i, 0:i] We can store A[0:i, 0:i] in shared memory (it's at most 64×64 = 16KB), and have each thread compute one or more j values. For j from 0 to i-1: dot_product of orig_A[i, 0:i] and A[0:i, j] With 64 threads, each thread handles one j. The dot product can be computed with a parallel reduction in shared memory. Actually, let me think about this differently. With 64 threads and 64×64 matrices: - Load the entire matrix into shared memory: 64×64 floats = 16KB. This fits easily in shared memory. - For each row i from 1 to 63: - Thread j computes dot(orig_A[i, 0:i], A[0:i, j]) for j < i - Thread j >= i does nothing - __syncthreads() - Update A[i, j] for j < i - __syncthreads() The dot product of length i can be computed by each thread independently in a loop. For i=64, each thread does 64 multiplies and adds. With 64 threads active for i=64, that's 4096 FLOPs. At ~10 TFLOPS for fp32, this is 0.4 microseconds per row, or ~25 microseconds for all 64 rows. With 512 blocks, total time would be ~0.013 ms. That's 75x faster than the loop! But wait, we need to read orig_A[i, 0:i] which is 64 floats. We can store this in a shared memory array or have each thread read from shared memory. Let me write this kernel.

command (2230 chars)

import torch
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

cuda_source = '''
__global__ void fwd_subst_kernel(float* __restrict__ A, int C, int batch) {
    int b = blockIdx.x;
    if (b >= batch) return;
    
    int tid = threadIdx.x;
    
    __shared__ float s_A[64][64];
    
    float* mat = A + b * C * C;
    
    // Load matrix into shared memory
    for (int idx = tid; idx < C * C; idx += blockDim.x) {
        s_A[idx / C][idx % C] = mat[idx];
    }
    __syncthreads();
    
    // Save original row i in a shared buffer
    __shared__ float s_row[64];
    
    for (int i = 1; i < C; i++) {
        // Save original row i
        if (tid < i) {
            s_row[tid] = s_A[i][tid];
        }
        __syncthreads();
        
        // Each thread computes one j
        if (tid < i) {
            float sum = 0.0f;
            for (int k = 0; k < i; k++) {
                sum += s_row[k] * s_A[k][tid];
            }
            s_A[i][tid] += sum;
        }
        __syncthreads();
    }
    
    // Store back
    for (int idx = tid; idx < C * C; idx += blockDim.x) {
        mat[idx] = s_A[idx / C][idx % C];
    }
}

torch::Tensor fwd_subst(torch::Tensor A) {
    int batch = A.size(0);
    int C = A.size(1);
    fwd_subst_kernel<<<batch, 64>>>(A.data_ptr<float>(), C, batch);
    return A;
}
'''

module = load_inline(
    name='fwd_subst2',
    cpp_sources='torch::Tensor fwd_subst(torch::Tensor A);',
    cuda_sources=cuda_source,
    functions=['fwd_subst'],
    extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3'],
    verbose=False,
)

C = 64
BHN = 512
A = torch.randn(BHN, C, C, device='cuda') * 0.1
mask = torch.tril(torch.ones(C, C, device='cuda'), diagonal=-1)
A = (A * mask).contiguous()

# Correctness
ref = A.clone()
for i in range(1, C):
    ref[:, i, :i] = ref[:, i, :i] + (ref[:, i, :, None] * ref[:, :, :i]).sum(-2)

out = module.fwd_subst(A.clone())
print('Max diff:', (ref - out).abs().max().item())

# Timing
torch.cuda.synchronize()
import time
start = time.time()
for _ in range(10000):
    _ = module.fwd_subst(A.clone())
torch.cuda.synchronize()
print(f'CUDA kernel (64 threads): {(time.time()-start)/10000*1000:.3f} ms')

Actually, for the inter-chunk part, let me try writing a CUDA kernel first and see how much speedup I can get. For the CUDA kernel, I need to handle the memory layout carefully. Let me define: - q: (B, T, H, K) - k: (B, T, H, K) - v: (B, T, H, V) - actually u is computed from v - g: (B, T, H, K) - beta: (B, T, H) - w: (B, T, H, K) - u: (B, T, H, V) - Aqk: (B, T, H, BT) - o: (B, T, H, V) Wait, in the reference, after reshaping, the layout is (B, H, N, C, K/V). But for the CUDA kernel, I can work with either layout. Let me use the (B, T, H, K) layout to avoid extra reshuffles. For q at position (b, t, h, k): q[b * T * H * K + t * H * K + h * K + k] For Aqk at position (b, t, h, j): Aqk[b * T * H * BT + t * H * BT + h * BT + j] For w at position (b, t, h, k): same as q. OK, let me write a CUDA kernel for the inter-chunk recurrence. Actually, let me take a step back. Writing multiple complex CUDA kernels from scratch might take a very long time. Let me see if there's a middle ground. What if I write the inter-chunk recurrence as a single Triton kernel, and keep the intra-chunk in PyTorch but with the CUDA forward substitution kernel? That might get me to ~1.5ms total, which is still 7.5x slower than SOTA but maybe acceptable for passing the benchmark? Wait, the benchmark says: ``` peak_fraction: <gmean> RESULT: OK if gmean >= 0.1 else LOW ``` So I need peak_fraction >= 0.1. For the headline shape (B=2,T=2048): - FLOPs = 4.3B - Peak = 200 TFLOPS - For peak_fraction = 0.1: Time = 4.3B / (200T * 0.1) = 4.3B / 20T = 0.215 ms The SOTA gets 0.202ms, which is about 0.107 peak fraction. So I need to be at most ~0.215ms for this shape to get OK. At 1.5ms, peak fraction = 4.3B / (200T * 0.0015) = 4.3 / 300 = 0.014 = 1.4%. That's way below 10%. For smaller shapes, the peak fraction will be even worse because there's more overhead relative to compute. So I really need to get close to SOTA performance. This means writing proper fused Triton kernels. Let me think about what the minimal set of kernels is: 1. One kernel for intra-chunk A_kk, forward substitution, w, u, Aqk 2. One kernel for inter-chunk recurrence + output Or even better: one mega-kernel for everything? Probably too complex. For kernel 1, the main operations are: - Compute A_kk = einsum(k*exp(g), k*exp(-g)) with masking - Forward substitution on A_kk - Compute w = A_kk @ (k*exp(g)) and u = A_kk @ v - Compute Aqk = einsum(q*exp(g), k*exp(-g)) with masking The einsum operations can be written as matmuls by transposing. For a chunk of 64 tokens: - k*exp(g) is (64, K) - k*exp(-g) is (64, K) - A_kk = (k*exp(g)) @ (k*exp(-g)).T -> (64, 64) This is just a standard matmul! With K=128, it's (64, 128) @ (128, 64) = (64, 64). Similarly for Aqk: (64, 128) @ (128, 64) = (64, 64). And w = A_kk @ (k*exp(g)): (64, 64) @ (64, 128) = (64, 128) u = A_kk @ v: (64, 64) @ (64, 128) = (64, 128) These are all standard matmuls! The only custom part is: 1. Applying the exp(g) and exp(-g) scaling 2. The forward substitution on the 64×64 matrix 3. The masking (lower triangular) For the forward substitution, we already have a fast CUDA kernel. But we could also write it in Triton. Actually, since all these operations are on 64×64 or 64×128 matrices, maybe I can write a single Triton kernel per chunk that does everything. Wait, with Triton, we can have each thread block handle one (B, H, chunk) and process the entire chunk. For 512 chunks, we'd have 512 thread blocks. Each thread block does: 1. Load k, g, beta, v, q for its 64 tokens 2. Compute A_kk using a 64×64 matmul 3. Do forward substitution (in shared memory or registers) 4. Compute w and u using matmuls 5. Compute Aqk using a matmul 6. Store w, u, Aqk The issue is that Triton's `tl.dot` uses Tensor Cores, which are great for large matmuls but might have high overhead for 64×64. However, with K=128, the matmul is 64×128 @ 128×64, which is reasonably sized for Tensor Cores. Actually, in Triton, for a single thread block doing a 64×128 @ 128×64 matmul, we'd need to tile it. With BK=64, we'd do 2 iterations of loading 64×64 tiles and accumulating. Let me look at how FLA's `chunk_kda_fwd_kernel_intra_sub_chunk` does it. They use sub-chunks of BC=16 within BT=64. This is because a 64×64 matmul doesn't fill the Tensor Cores well on some architectures, but 16×16 or 16×64 does. For Blackwell (SM120), Tensor Cores might handle 64×64 better, but I'm not sure. Let me try a different approach. Instead of writing everything from scratch, let me see if I can write a solution that: 1. Uses PyTorch for the matmuls and einsums 2. Uses my custom CUDA kernel for forward substitution 3. Uses `torch.compile` on the inter-chunk loop with some optimizations Actually, `torch.compile` might not help with the sequential loop. What if I rewrite the inter-chunk loop using `torch.scan` or associative scan? For linear recurrences, there are parallel scan algorithms. But KDA's recurrence is not a simple associative scan because it involves matrix multiplications. Actually, let me re-examine the inter-chunk recurrence. For each chunk: 1. v_i = u_i - w_i @ S 2. o_i = (q_i * exp(g_i)) @ S + Aqk @ v_i 3. S = S * decay + k_decay.T @ v_i This can be written as: ``` S_{i+1} = S_i * decay_i + k_decay_i.T @ (u_i - w_i @ S_i) = S_i * decay_i + k_decay_i.T @ u_i - k_decay_i.T @ w_i @ S_i = (decay_i - k_decay_i.T @ w_i) @ S_i + k_decay_i.T @ u_i ``` Wait, `decay_i` is a diagonal matrix (or elementwise multiplication). And `k_decay_i.T @ w_i` is (K, C) @ (C, K) = (K, K). So: ``` S_{i+1} = diag(decay_i) @ S_i - (k_decay_i.T @ w_i) @ S_i + k_decay_i.T @ u_i = (diag(decay_i) - k_decay_i.T @ w_i) @ S_i + k_decay_i.T @ u_i ``` This is a linear recurrence! And it CAN be parallelized using associative scan if we express it as: ``` S_{i+1} = M_i @ S_i + B_i ``` Where M_i = diag(decay_i) - k_decay_i.T @ w_i is (K, K), and B_i = k_decay_i.T @ u_i is (K, V). For a linear recurrence S_{i+1} = M_i S_i + B_i, there's a parallel scan algorithm: - We can write it as an affine transformation: [S_{i+1}; I] = [M_i, B_i; 0, I] @ [S_i; I] - Then the scan is just a matrix product scan over the 2K×2K matrices... wait, that's (K+V)×(K+V) which is 256×256. For 32 chunks, scanning 256×256 matrices is expensive. But there is a more efficient approach using the parallel prefix algorithm for linear recurrences. However, implementing this in PyTorch/Triton is complex. For simplicity, let me just write a Triton kernel for the sequential inter-chunk recurrence. With proper tiling, it should be much faster than the PyTorch loop. Let me write a focused Triton kernel. I'll base it on FLA's `chunk_gated_delta_rule_fwd_kernel_h_blockdim64` but add the output computation. Actually, looking at FLA's code more carefully, they have two separate kernels: 1. `chunk_gated_delta_rule_fwd_kernel_h_blockdim64` computes h (states) and v_new 2. `chunk_gla_fwd_kernel_o` computes o from h, v_new, q, g, A The reason they split is because h has shape (B, NT, H, K, V), which is large: for B=2, NT=32, H=8, K=128, V=128, h is 2*32*8*128*128 = 8M floats = 32MB. They save h for the backward pass. For inference (our case, since we only need forward), we could fuse these two kernels. But writing a fused kernel is more complex. Let me try to write just the inter-chunk state kernel in Triton, similar to FLA's, and then do the output computation either in the same kernel or in a separate one. Actually, let me look at what `recompute_w_u_fwd` does in FLA. It's in `wy_fast.py`.

import torch
import reference
import shapes

shape = shapes.SHAPES[1]
for k, v in shape.items():
    setattr(reference, k, v)

torch.manual_seed(2026)
inputs = [t.cuda() if hasattr(t, 'cuda') else t for t in reference.get_inputs()]
q, k, v, g, beta = inputs
scale = float(shape['K']) ** -0.5

B, T, H, K = q.shape
V = v.shape[-1]
BT = 64
NT = T // BT

qf = q.float() * scale
kf = k.float()
vf = v.float()
gf = g.float()
bf = beta.float()

qf = qf.view(B, NT, BT, H, K).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4)
kf = kf.view(B, NT, BT, H, K).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4)
vf = vf.view(B, NT, BT, H, V).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4)
gf = gf.view(B, NT, BT, H, K).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4)
bf = bf.view(B, NT, BT, H).permute(0, 3, 1, 2)

mask_upper = torch.triu(torch.ones(BT, BT, device=q.device, dtype=torch.bool), diagonal=0)
mask_strict = torch.triu(torch.ones(BT, BT, device=q.device, dtype=torch.bool), diagonal=1)
I = torch.eye(BT, device=q.device, dtype=torch.float32)

gf = gf.cumsum(-2)

# Measure with cuda events
start_event = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
end_event = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)

# Warmup
for _ in range(10):
    kg = kf * gf.exp()
    kgn = kf * (-gf).exp()
    A = torch.einsum('bhnik,bhnjk->bhnij', kg, kgn)
    A = A * bf[..., None]
    A = -A.masked_fill(mask_upper, 0)
    for i in range(1, BT):
        A[..., i, :i] = A[..., i, :i] + (A[..., i, :, None] * A[..., :, :i]).sum(-2)
    A = (A + I) * bf[..., None, :]
    w = torch.matmul(A, (gf.exp() * kf))
    u = torch.matmul(A, vf)
    qg = qf * gf.exp()
    Aqk = torch.einsum('bhnik,bhnjk->bhnij', qg, kgn)
    Aqk = Aqk.masked_fill(mask_strict, 0)

torch.cuda.synchronize()
start_event.record()
for _ in range(100):
    kg = kf * gf.exp()
    kgn = kf * (-gf).exp()
    A = torch.einsum('bhnik,bhnjk->bhnij', kg, kgn)
    A = A * bf[..., None]
    A = -A.masked_fill(mask_upper, 0)
    for i in range(1, BT):
        A[..., i, :i] = A[..., i, :i] + (A[..., i, :, None] * A[..., :, :i]).sum(-2)
    A = (A + I) * bf[..., None, :]
    w = torch.matmul(A, (gf.exp() * kf))
    u = torch.matmul(A, vf)
    qg = qf * gf.exp()
    Aqk = torch.einsum('bhnik,bhnjk->bhnij', qg, kgn)
    Aqk = Aqk.masked_fill(mask_strict, 0)
end_event.record()
torch.cuda.synchronize()
print(f'Intra-chunk (100 iters): {start_event.elapsed_time(end_event):.3f} ms')
print(f'Intra-chunk per iter: {start_event.elapsed_time(end_event)/100:.3f} ms')

# Measure just einsum + matmul without fwd subst
start_event.record()
for _ in range(100):
    kg = kf * gf.exp()
    kgn = kf * (-gf).exp()
    A = torch.einsum('bhnik,bhnjk->bhnij', kg, kgn)
    A = A * bf[..., None]
    A = -A.masked_fill(mask_upper, 0)
    A = (A + I) * bf[..., None, :]
    w = torch.matmul(A, (gf.exp() * kf))
    u = torch.matmul(A, vf)
    qg = qf * gf.exp()
    Aqk = torch.einsum('bhnik,bhnjk->bhnij', qg, kgn)
    Aqk = Aqk.masked_fill(mask_strict, 0)
end_event.record()
torch.cuda.synchronize()
print(f'Without fwd subst (100 iters): {start_event.elapsed_time(end_event):.3f} ms')
print(f'Without fwd subst per iter: {start_event.elapsed_time(end_event)/100:.3f} ms')

u = torch.matmul(A, vf)
qg = qf * gf.exp()
Aqk = torch.einsum('bhnik,bhnjk->bhnij', qg, kgn)
Aqk = Aqk.masked_fill(mask_strict, 0)

def inter_chunk(qf, kf, gf, w, u, Aqk):
    S = qf.new_zeros(B, H, K, V)
    o = torch.zeros_like(vf)
    for i in range(NT):
        q_i = qf[:, :, i]
        k_i = kf[:, :, i]
        u_i = u[:, :, i]
        g_i = gf[:, :, i]
        w_i = w[:, :, i]
        
        v_i = u_i - torch.matmul(w_i, S)
        qS = torch.matmul(q_i * g_i.exp(), S)
        Av = torch.matmul(Aqk[:, :, i], v_i)
        o[:, :, i] = qS + Av
        
        decay = g_i[:, :, -1].exp().unsqueeze(-1)
        S = S * decay
        
        kg_decay = (g_i[:, :, -1:] - g_i).exp() * k_i
        S = S + torch.matmul(kg_decay.transpose(-2, -1), v_i)
    return o

compiled_inter = torch.compile(inter_chunk, mode='max-autotune')

# Warmup compiled
for _ in range(5):
    _ = compiled_inter(qf, kf, gf, w, u, Aqk)

start_event = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
end_event = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
start_event.record()
for _ in range(100):
    _ = compiled_inter(qf, kf, gf, w, u, Aqk)
end_event.record()
torch.cuda.synchronize()
print(f'Compiled inter-chunk (100 iters): {start_event.elapsed_time(end_event):.3f} ms')
print(f'Compiled inter-chunk per iter: {start_event.elapsed_time(end_event)/100:.3f} ms')

# Baseline
start_event.record()
for _ in range(100):
    _ = inter_chunk(qf, kf, gf, w, u, Aqk)
end_event.record()
torch.cuda.synchronize()
print(f'Eager inter-chunk (100 iters): {start_event.elapsed_time(end_event):.3f} ms')
print(f'Eager inter-chunk per iter: {start_event.elapsed_time(end_event)/100:.3f} ms')
