The `torch.compile` is taking a very long time due to autotuning and seems to have some issues with out-of-resource errors on Blackwell. Let me kill this and take a different approach. I need to write custom Triton kernels. Let me focus on writing a solution with: 1. PyTorch for the initial reshape and cumsum 2. A custom Triton kernel for the inter-chunk recurrence and output 3. The intra-chunk can stay in optimized PyTorch + my CUDA forward substitution kernel Actually, let me reconsider. The SOTA gets 0.2ms. To get even close to 0.2ms, I need highly optimized kernels for ALL parts. But writing multiple complex Triton kernels from scratch is extremely time-consuming. Let me look at this from a different angle. What if I write a single CUDA kernel for the ENTIRE KDA forward pass? For each (B, H, chunk), I need to: 1. Load q, k, v, g, beta for 64 tokens 2. Compute A_kk and Aqk 3. Do forward substitution on A_kk 4. Compute w and u 5. For the inter-chunk part, I need state from previous chunks The inter-chunk dependency means I can't parallelize across chunks within a single (B, H). But I CAN process chunks sequentially within a thread block. A single mega-kernel per (B, H) would: - Initialize S = zeros(K, V) - For each chunk i from 0 to NT-1: - Load inputs for chunk i - Compute A_kk, Aqk intra-chunk - Forward substitution on A_kk - Compute w, u - Compute v_i = u_i - w_i @ S - Compute o_i = (q_i * exp(g_i)) @ S + Aqk @ v_i - Store o_i - Update S This is a LOT of code for one kernel, but it eliminates all intermediate kernel launches and memory traffic. For K=V=128, S is 128×128 = 16K fp32 = 64KB. With shared memory limit of ~228KB, this fits easily. The inputs per chunk are: - q: 64×128 = 8K - k: 64×128 = 8K - v: 64×128 = 8K - g: 64×128 = 8K (fp32 = 16K) - beta: 64 - A_kk: 64×64 = 4K - Aqk: 64×64 = 4K Total shared memory: 64KB (S) + 8+8+8+16+0.25+4+4 = 112KB. This is within limits. But writing this entire kernel in CUDA is complex. In Triton, we can't easily do the sequential forward substitution on a 64×64 matrix within the same kernel because Triton doesn't have good support for sequential loops with complex control flow. Wait, actually we CAN do it in Triton. Let me think... In Triton, within a kernel, we can have a `for` loop over chunks. For each chunk: 1. Load q, k, v, g, beta tiles 2. Compute A_kk using `tl.dot` 3. Do forward substitution using a sequential loop with `tl.dot` for matrix updates 4. Compute w, u, v_i, o_i 5. Update S The issue is that the forward substitution on a 64×64 matrix requires sequential row updates. In Triton, if each thread block handles one chunk, we can do this in registers/shared memory. But Triton is designed for parallelizing over large dimensions, not for sequential algorithms on small matrices. A CUDA kernel might be more appropriate here. Actually, let me try a pragmatic approach. I'll write: 1. A CUDA kernel for the forward substitution (already done, 0.034ms) 2. A Triton kernel for the inter-chunk recurrence 3. Optimized PyTorch for everything else For the inter-chunk Triton kernel, I'll write it as a single kernel that processes all chunks sequentially per (B, H, V_tile). Let me write this kernel now. I'll base it heavily on FLA's `chunk_gated_delta_rule_fwd_kernel_h_blockdim64` and `chunk_gla_fwd_kernel_o`.

+import triton

+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+# CUDA forward substitution kernel (loaded inline)
+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+_CUDA_FWD_SUBST_SRC = """
+__global__ void fwd_subst_kernel(float* __restrict__ A, int C, int batch) {
+    int b = blockIdx.x;
+    if (b >= batch) return;
+    int tid = threadIdx.x;
+    __shared__ float s_A[64][64];
+    __shared__ float s_row[64];
+    float* mat = A + b * C * C;
+    for (int idx = tid; idx < C * C; idx += blockDim.x) {
+        s_A[idx / C][idx % C] = mat[idx];
+    }
+    __syncthreads();
+    for (int i = 1; i < C; i++) {
+        if (tid < i) s_row[tid] = s_A[i][tid];
+        __syncthreads();
+        if (tid < i) {
+            float sum = 0.0f;
+            for (int k = 0; k < i; k++) {
+                sum += s_row[k] * s_A[k][tid];
+            }
+            s_A[i][tid] += sum;
+        }
+        __syncthreads();
+    }
+    for (int idx = tid; idx < C * C; idx += blockDim.x) {
+        mat[idx] = s_A[idx / C][idx % C];
+    }
+}
+torch::Tensor fwd_subst(torch::Tensor A) {
+    int batch = A.size(0);
+    int C = A.size(1);
+    fwd_subst_kernel<<<batch, 64>>>(A.data_ptr<float>(), C, batch);
+    return A;
+}
+"""
+
+_fwd_subst_mod = torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline(
+    name="kda_fwd_subst",
+    cpp_sources="torch::Tensor fwd_subst(torch::Tensor A);",

+    functions=["fwd_subst"],
+    extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3"],
+    verbose=False,

+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+# Triton kernel: inter-chunk recurrence + output
+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+@triton.jit
+def kda_inter_chunk_kernel(
+    q_ptr,
+    k_ptr,
+    u_ptr,
+    w_ptr,
+    g_ptr,
+    Aqk_ptr,
+    o_ptr,
+    B: tl.constexpr,
+    T: tl.constexpr,
+    H: tl.constexpr,
+    K: tl.constexpr,
+    V: tl.constexpr,
+    BT: tl.constexpr,
+    BV: tl.constexpr,
+    scale: tl.constexpr,
+):
+    i_v = tl.program_id(0)
+    i_bh = tl.program_id(1)
+    i_b = i_bh // H
+    i_h = i_bh % H
+
+    NT = T // BT
+
+    # State tiles: K is fixed at 128, split into two 64-wide tiles
+    # S1: (64, BV), S2: (64, BV)
+    b_S1 = tl.zeros((64, BV), dtype=tl.float32)
+    b_S2 = tl.zeros((64, BV), dtype=tl.float32)
+
+    # Pointers base
+    q_base = q_ptr + (i_b * T * H + i_h) * K
+    k_base = k_ptr + (i_b * T * H + i_h) * K
+    u_base = u_ptr + (i_b * T * H + i_h) * V
+    w_base = w_ptr + (i_b * T * H + i_h) * K
+    g_base = g_ptr + (i_b * T * H + i_h) * K
+    Aqk_base = Aqk_ptr + (i_b * T * H + i_h) * BT
+    o_base = o_ptr + (i_b * T * H + i_h) * V
+
+    for i_t in range(NT):
+        t0 = i_t * BT
+
+        # Load u_i tile (BT, BV)
+        p_u = tl.make_block_ptr(
+            u_base, (T, V), (H * V, 1), (t0, i_v * BV), (BT, BV), (1, 0)
+        )
+        b_v = tl.load(p_u, boundary_check=(0, 1)).to(tl.float32)
+
+        # v_i = u_i - w_i @ S
+        # First K tile (0:64)
+        p_w = tl.make_block_ptr(
+            w_base, (T, K), (H * K, 1), (t0, 0), (BT, 64), (1, 0)
+        )
+        b_w = tl.load(p_w, boundary_check=(0, 1))
+        b_v -= tl.dot(b_w, b_S1.to(b_w.dtype))
+
+        # Second K tile (64:128)
+        p_w = tl.make_block_ptr(
+            w_base, (T, K), (H * K, 1), (t0, 64), (BT, 64), (1, 0)
+        )
+        b_w = tl.load(p_w, boundary_check=(0, 1))
+        b_v -= tl.dot(b_w, b_S2.to(b_w.dtype))
+
+        # Compute o_i = (q_i * exp(g_i)) @ S + Aqk @ v_i
+        b_o = tl.zeros((BT, BV), dtype=tl.float32)
+
+        # qS from first K tile
+        p_q = tl.make_block_ptr(
+            q_base, (T, K), (H * K, 1), (t0, 0), (BT, 64), (1, 0)
+        )
+        p_g = tl.make_block_ptr(
+            g_base, (T, K), (H * K, 1), (t0, 0), (BT, 64), (1, 0)
+        )
+        b_q = tl.load(p_q, boundary_check=(0, 1))
+        b_g = tl.load(p_g, boundary_check=(0, 1)).to(tl.float32)
+        b_qg = (b_q * tl.exp(b_g)).to(b_q.dtype)
+        b_o += tl.dot(b_qg, b_S1.to(b_qg.dtype))
+
+        # qS from second K tile
+        p_q = tl.make_block_ptr(
+            q_base, (T, K), (H * K, 1), (t0, 64), (BT, 64), (1, 0)
+        )
+        p_g = tl.make_block_ptr(
+            g_base, (T, K), (H * K, 1), (t0, 64), (BT, 64), (1, 0)
+        )
+        b_q = tl.load(p_q, boundary_check=(0, 1))
+        b_g = tl.load(p_g, boundary_check=(0, 1)).to(tl.float32)
+        b_qg = (b_q * tl.exp(b_g)).to(b_q.dtype)
+        b_o += tl.dot(b_qg, b_S2.to(b_qg.dtype))
+
+        b_o *= scale
+
+        # Aqk @ v_i
+        p_Aqk = tl.make_block_ptr(
+            Aqk_base, (T, BT), (H * BT, 1), (t0, 0), (BT, BT), (1, 0)
+        )
+        b_Aqk = tl.load(p_Aqk, boundary_check=(0, 1))
+        # Mask to strict lower triangular
+        o_i = tl.arange(0, BT)
+        m_s = o_i[:, None] >= o_i[None, :]
+        b_Aqk = tl.where(m_s, b_Aqk, 0.0).to(b_v.dtype)
+        b_o += tl.dot(b_Aqk, b_v.to(b_Aqk.dtype))
+
+        # Store o_i
+        p_o = tl.make_block_ptr(
+            o_base, (T, V), (H * V, 1), (t0, i_v * BV), (BT, BV), (1, 0)
+        )
+        tl.store(p_o, b_o.to(o_ptr.dtype.element_ty), boundary_check=(0, 1))
+
+        # Update state S
+        # decay = exp(g_i[:, -1])
+        last_idx = t0 + BT - 1
+        p_glast1 = g_base + last_idx * H * K + tl.arange(0, 64)
+        p_glast2 = g_base + last_idx * H * K + 64 + tl.arange(0, 64)
+        b_glast1

import torch
import reference
import solution
import shapes

device = torch.device('cuda:0')
for shape_idx, shape in enumerate(shapes.SHAPES):
    for k, v in shape.items():
        setattr(reference, k, v)
    
    init_args = reference.get_init_inputs()
    ref_model = reference.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
    sol_model = solution.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
    
    torch.manual_seed(2026)
    inputs = [t.to(device) if hasattr(t, 'to') else t for t in reference.get_inputs()]
    
    # Warmup
    with torch.no_grad():
        for _ in range(5):
            _ = sol_model(*inputs)
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    
    # Time
    start = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    end = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    start.record()
    with torch.no_grad():
        for _ in range(50):
            _ = sol_model(*inputs)
    end.record()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    ms = start.elapsed_time(end) / 50
    
    # FLOPs
    B, T, H, K, V = shape['B'], shape['T'], shape['H'], shape['K'], shape['V']
    CHUNK_SIZE = shape['CHUNK_SIZE']
    flops = 4 * B * T * H * (K * V + CHUNK_SIZE * K + CHUNK_SIZE * V)
    tflops = flops / (ms * 1e-3) / 1e12
    peak_frac = tflops / 200.0
    
    print(f'Shape {shape_idx}: {ms:.3f} ms, {tflops:.2f} TFLOPS, peak_fraction={peak_frac:.4f}')

The Triton kernel takes 0.48ms per call, which is still quite slow. It should be much faster. Let me see if I can optimize it. Looking at the kernel, I have `num_warps=4, num_stages=1`. But the kernel might still be using too many registers or not saturating the GPU. Let me think about why the Triton kernel is slow. For B=2, H=8, V_tiles=2, we have 32 thread blocks. Each block processes 32 chunks (for T=2048). With 4 warps per block = 128 threads, that's 32 * 128 = 4096 threads total. For each chunk, the kernel does: 1. Load u_i (64×64) = 4096 floats 2. Load w_i (64×64) twice = 8192 floats 3. Two matmuls for v_i: each is (64,64) @ (64,64) = 2 × 262K FLOPs 4. Load q_i, g_i twice = 8192 floats 5. Two matmuls for qS = 2 × 262K FLOPs 6. Two matmuls for state update = 2 × 262K FLOPs Total per chunk: ~40K floats loaded/stored + ~1.3M FLOPs For 32 chunks: ~1.3M floats + ~42M FLOPs At 1.8TB/s, 1.3M floats (5.2MB) takes ~2.9 microseconds At 200 TFLOPS, 42M FLOPs takes ~0.2 microseconds So the theoretical minimum is ~3 microseconds per block, or ~0.003ms total for all 32 blocks. But we're getting 0.48ms, which is 160x slower! This suggests the kernel is not well-optimized. The issue might be: 1. Small number of thread blocks (32) doesn't saturate the GPU 2. Register spilling or poor instruction scheduling 3. Memory access patterns With only 32 thread blocks on a GPU with many SMs (RTX PRO 6000 has ~120 SMs?), most SMs are idle. Each thread block processes 32 chunks sequentially, but there are only 32 thread blocks total. Wait, for B=2, H=8, V_tiles=2: grid = (2, 16). Yes, only 32 thread blocks. That's very few for a GPU with 120+ SMs. The solution is to increase parallelism. Instead of having each thread block process all chunks sequentially, we can have more thread blocks by processing chunks in parallel or using smaller V tiles. But the inter-chunk recurrence is sequential, so we can't parallelize across chunks. However, we can increase the number of thread blocks by using smaller V tiles. With BV=32, we'd have 64 thread blocks. With BV=16, we'd have 128 thread blocks. Let me try BV=32. Actually, there's another issue. With only 4 warps per block (128 threads), and each block doing 32 chunks, the GPU might not have enough occupancy. Let me also try increasing num_warps to 8. Another optimization: in the kernel, I'm doing `b_S1 = tl.zeros((64, BV), dtype=tl.float32)`. For BV=64, this is 4096 fp32 values. The compiler might put these in shared memory or registers. With 128 threads, if each thread holds 32 fp32 values, that's manageable. Let me try different configurations. Actually, let me also check if the slow performance is because Triton is compiling the kernel slowly. The first call might take longer. Let me measure with more warmup iterations. Wait, looking at the profiler again: `kda_inter_chunk_kernel` shows 480.761us per call. For 32 blocks, that's 480us / 32 = 15us per block. With 32 chunks per block, that's 15us / 32 = 0.47us per chunk. Actually, the kernel time is the total time across all blocks. Since blocks run in parallel, 480us is the wall-clock time for all 32 blocks to finish. With 32 blocks on a 120-SM GPU, most blocks run immediately without queuing. So the wall-clock time per block is roughly 480us. With 32 chunks per block, each chunk takes 480us / 32 = 15us. That's still very slow for what should be ~3us of memory traffic per chunk. The issue might be that with num_warps=4 and num_stages=1, Triton is not using Tensor Cores efficiently for the small matmuls. The 64×64 @ 64×64 matmuls might be falling back to SIMT (CUDA cores) instead of Tensor Cores, which is much slower. On Blackwell, the Tensor Cores might need larger tile sizes to be efficient. Or Triton might not be generating optimal code for SM120 yet. Let me try using `num_warps=8` to give Triton more threads to work with. import torch
import reference
import solution
import shapes

device = torch.device('cuda:0')
shape = shapes.SHAPES[1]
for k, v in shape.items():
    setattr(reference, k, v)

init_args = reference.get_init_inputs()
sol_model = solution.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()

torch.manual_seed(2026)
inputs = [t.to(device) if hasattr(t, 'to') else t for t in reference.get_inputs()]

# Warmup
with torch.no_grad():
    for _ in range(10):
        _ = sol_model(*inputs)

torch.cuda.synchronize()

# Time with different num_warps by modifying the kernel launch
# Actually, let's just test num_warps=8 directly
from solution import kda_inter_chunk_kernel

B, T, H, K, V = shape['B'], shape['T'], shape['H'], shape['K'], shape['V']
BT = shape['CHUNK_SIZE']
BV = 64

# Get the intermediate tensors
with torch.no_grad():
    # Run forward once to get tensors
    qf = inputs[0].float() * (float(K) ** -0.5)
    kf = inputs[1].float()
    vf = inputs[2].float()
    gf = inputs[3].float()
    bf = inputs[4].float()
    
    B_, T_, H_, K_ = qf.shape
    V_ = vf.shape[-1]
    NT_ = T_ // BT
    
    qf = qf.view(B_, NT_, BT, H_, K_).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4)
    kf = kf.view(B_, NT_, BT, H_, K_).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4)
    vf = vf.view(B_, NT_, BT, H_, V_).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4)
    gf = gf.view(B_, NT_, BT, H_, K_).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4)
    bf = bf.view(B_, NT_, BT, H_).permute(0, 3, 1, 2)
    
    gf = gf.cumsum(-2)
    
    kg = kf * gf.exp()
    kgn = kf * (-gf).exp()
    BHN = B_ * H_ * NT_
    kg_flat = kg.reshape(BHN, BT, K_)
    kgn_flat = kgn.reshape(BHN, BT, K_)
    A = torch.bmm(kg_flat, kgn_flat.transpose(-2, -1)).view(B_, H_, NT_, BT, BT)
    mask_upper = torch.triu(torch.ones(BT, BT, device=device, dtype=torch.bool), diagonal=0)
    A = A * bf[..., None]
    A = -A.masked_fill(mask_upper, 0)
    A_flat = A.view(BHN, BT, BT).contiguous()
    A_flat = solution._fwd_subst_mod.fwd_subst(A_flat)
    A = A_flat.view(B_, H_, NT_, BT, BT)
    I = torch.eye(BT, device=device, dtype=torch.float32)
    A = (A + I) * bf[..., None, :]
    w = torch.matmul(A, (gf.exp() * kf))
    u = torch.matmul(A, vf)
    qg = qf * gf.exp()
    qg_flat = qg.reshape(BHN, BT, K_)
    Aqk = torch.bmm(qg_flat, kgn_flat.transpose(-2, -1)).view(B_, H_, NT_, BT, BT)
    mask_strict = torch.triu(torch.ones(BT, BT, device=device, dtype=torch.bool), diagonal=1)
    Aqk = Aqk.masked_fill(mask_strict, 0)
    
    q_flat = qf.permute(0, 2, 3, 4, 1).reshape(B_, T_, H_, K_)
    k_flat = kf.permute(0, 2, 3, 4, 1).reshape(B_, T_, H_, K_)
    u_flat = u.permute(0, 2, 3, 4, 1).reshape(B_, T_, H_, V_)
    w_flat = w.permute(0, 2, 3, 4, 1).reshape(B_, T_, H_, K_)
    g_flat = gf.permute(0, 2, 3, 4, 1).reshape(B_, T_, H_, K_)
    Aqk_flat = Aqk.permute(0, 2, 3, 4, 1).reshape(B_, T_, H_, BT)
    o = torch.empty_like(inputs[2])
    
    scale = float(K_) ** -0.5
    
    # Test with num_warps=8
    grid = (triton.cdiv(V_, BV), B_ * H_)
    kda_inter_chunk_kernel[grid](
        q_flat, k_flat, u_flat, w_flat, g_flat, Aqk_flat, o,
        B=B_, T=T_, H=H_, K=K_, V=V_, BT=BT, BV=BV, scale=scale,
        num_warps=8, num_stages=1,
    )
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    
    start = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    end = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    start.record()
    for _ in range(100):
        kda_inter_chunk_kernel[grid](
            q_flat, k_flat, u_flat, w_flat, g_flat, Aqk_flat, o,
            B=B_, T=T_, H=H_, K=K_, V=V_, BT=BT, BV=BV, scale=scale,
            num_warps=8, num_stages=1,
        )
    end.record()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    print(f'num_warps=8: {start.elapsed_time(end)/100:.4f} ms')
    
    # Test with num_warps=4, BV=32

    grid2 = (triton.cdiv(V_, BV2), B_ * H_)
    kda_inter_chunk_kernel[grid2](

reasoning (4177 chars)
BV=32 with num_warps=4 gives 0.247ms, which is much better than BV=64 with num_warps=4 (0.48ms). But BV=32 with num_warps=8 is terrible (4.25ms), probably due to register spilling. So BV=32, num_warps=4 is the sweet spot for the inter-chunk kernel. That's about 2x faster than before. Now let me update the solution to use BV=32 and num_warps=4, and see what the overall performance looks like. Also, I notice that the inter-chunk kernel still dominates. The total with BV=32 would be roughly: - Intra-chunk: ~0.2ms - Inter-chunk: ~0.25ms - Total: ~0.45ms For the headline shape, that's 0.45ms. FLOPs = 4.3B. TFLOPS = 4.3 / 0.00045 = 9.6 TFLOPS. Peak fraction = 9.6 / 200 = 4.8%. Still below 10%. For smaller shapes, it will be worse. Let me measure the actual total. But first, let me check if there are other optimizations I can make. Looking at the profiler output from earlier, there were many `aten::copy_` and `aten::clone` operations taking significant time. I should eliminate unnecessary copies. Let me also think about whether I can optimize the intra-chunk part further. The einsum/bmm takes ~0.2ms. Can I write a Triton kernel for this too? Actually, looking at FLA's performance (0.2ms total), my intra-chunk alone is about the same as their total! So there must be something fundamentally more efficient about their approach. Let me look at how FLA structures their intra-chunk computation. They have `chunk_kda_fwd_intra` which calls two kernels: 1. `chunk_kda_fwd_intra_token_parallel` - computes diagonal blocks 2. `chunk_kda_fwd_kernel_inter_solve_fused` - computes off-diagonal blocks + forward substitution These kernels operate on sub-chunks of size 16 within the chunk of 64. This allows better parallelism and Tensor Core utilization. For my solution, the main issue with the intra-chunk einsum is that I'm computing the FULL 64×64 matrix and then masking. A lot of compute is wasted on the upper triangular part. FLA's approach: 1. Compute diagonal 16×16 blocks directly 2. Compute off-diagonal blocks between sub-chunks 3. Do forward substitution on the diagonal blocks 4. Merge everything This is much more efficient because: - The diagonal blocks are computed with precise masking - The off-diagonal blocks are computed with block-wise matmuls - No wasted compute on the upper triangular But implementing this from scratch is very complex. Another approach: instead of computing A with einsum over the full 64×64, I can use a more efficient PyTorch operation. For example, `torch.tril` with `diagonal=-1` can be applied AFTER the matmul, but the matmul still computes all elements. Wait, I already do this. The issue is that for a 64×64 matmul with batch 512, even computing all elements is fast (0.2ms). The actual problem might be elsewhere. Let me look at the profiler output again. For the full solution, there were many `aten::copy_` operations. Let me trace where these come from. In my solution: ```python qf = qf.view(B, NT, BT, H, K).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4) ``` This creates a non-contiguous tensor. Then when I do: ```python kg_flat = kg.reshape(BHN, BT, K) ``` `reshape` might need to copy if the tensor is not contiguous in that dimension. Let me make the tensors contiguous after permutation. Also, in the Triton kernel, I flatten back: ```python q_flat = qf.permute(0, 2, 3, 4, 1).reshape(B, T, H, K) ``` This is also non-contiguous. But the Triton kernel reads with block pointers, so it might not matter much. Let me optimize the PyTorch part by using `contiguous()` after permutations. Actually, the bigger issue is that for each forward pass, I'm doing many operations: 1. `qf.view().permute()` 2. `gf.cumsum(-2)` 3. `kf * gf.exp()` - elementwise 4. `torch.bmm()` for A 5. `masked_fill` 6. `fwd_subst` 7. `matmul` for w and u 8. More `bmm` for Aqk 9. `permute().reshape()` for Triton kernel inputs 10. Triton kernel Many of these create intermediate tensors and do memory copies. For better performance, I should try to fuse more operations. But fusing everything into one kernel is hard. Let me first update the solution with BV=32 and see the overall performance. Then I'll look for more optimizations.

import torch
import reference
import solution
import shapes

device = torch.device('cuda:0')
for shape_idx, shape in enumerate(shapes.SHAPES):
    for k, v in shape.items():
        setattr(reference, k, v)
    
    init_args = reference.get_init_inputs()
    sol_model = solution.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
    
    torch.manual_seed(2026)
    inputs = [t.to(device) if hasattr(t, 'to') else t for t in reference.get_inputs()]
    
    # Warmup
    with torch.no_grad():
        for _ in range(10):
            _ = sol_model(*inputs)
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    
    # Time
    start = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    end = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    start.record()
    with torch.no_grad():
        for _ in range(50):
            _ = sol_model(*inputs)
    end.record()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    ms = start.elapsed_time(end) / 50
    
    # FLOPs
    B, T, H, K, V = shape['B'], shape['T'], shape['H'], shape['K'], shape['V']
    CHUNK_SIZE = shape['CHUNK_SIZE']
    flops = 4 * B * T * H * (K * V + CHUNK_SIZE * K + CHUNK_SIZE * V)
    tflops = flops / (ms * 1e-3) / 1e12
    peak_frac = tflops / 200.0
    
    print(f'Shape {shape_idx}: {ms:.3f} ms, {tflops:.2f} TFLOPS, peak_fraction={peak_frac:.4f}')

Actually, `masked_fill` on a contiguous tensor should be in-place or return a new contiguous tensor. Actually, looking at PyTorch docs, `masked_fill` returns a new tensor. So it always copies. We can use `masked_fill_` for in-place operation. Similarly, `cumsum` returns a new tensor. We can try to avoid this by using in-place operations where possible. Let me optimize the PyTorch code: 1. Use `.contiguous()` after permute 2. Use `masked_fill_` instead of `masked_fill` 3. Use in-place operations where possible But these are micro-optimizations. The real bottleneck is probably the Triton kernel and the matmul operations. Let me think about a more radical optimization. What if I write a single Triton kernel for the ENTIRE forward pass? For each thread block handling one (B, H, V_tile), I would: 1. Initialize state S = zeros(K, V_tile) 2. For each chunk: a. Load q, k, v, g, beta for the chunk b. Compute A_kk intra-chunk c. Forward substitution on A_kk d. Compute w, u e. Compute output and update state The issue is that steps b and c (intra-chunk) are complex to implement in Triton. But for K=128, C=64, it's manageable. Actually, looking at FLA's code, they have a separate kernel for intra-chunk because it's parallelizable across chunks. The inter-chunk is sequential. So fusing everything into one kernel would lose the parallelism of intra-chunk computations. But for our problem, with B*H*N = 512 chunks, the intra-chunk parallelism is decent. The inter-chunk has only 32 thread blocks (for B=2, H=8, V_tile=2). Wait, what if I process the intra-chunk computations in the SAME kernel but launch more thread blocks? Actually, for the inter-chunk kernel, each thread block processes all chunks sequentially. But the intra-chunk computations for different chunks are independent. So I can't easily parallelize them within a single thread block. What if I launch one thread block per (B, H, chunk, V_tile)? Then I'd have 512*2 = 1024 thread blocks for T=2048. But then the state S would need to be passed between thread blocks, which requires global memory. Actually, FLA's approach is: 1. Intra-chunk kernel: one block per chunk, computes A, w, u 2. Inter-chunk kernel: one block per (B, H, V_tile), sequential over chunks, computes output This is the optimal approach for parallelism. FLA just has much more optimized kernels. For my solution, the inter-chunk kernel is the bottleneck at 0.28ms. Can I optimize it further? Let me look at what the kernel is actually doing and see if I can reduce the number of matmuls or improve the tiling. In the kernel, for each chunk, I do: 1. `tl.dot(b_w, b_S1)` - (64, 64) @ (64, 32) 2. `tl.dot(b_w, b_S2)` - (64, 64) @ (64, 32) 3. `tl.dot(b_qg, b_S1)` - (64, 64) @ (64, 32) 4. `tl.dot(b_qg, b_S2)` - (64, 64) @ (64, 32) 5. `tl.dot(b_Aqk, b_v)` - (64, 64) @ (64, 32) 6. `tl.dot(b_kg.T, b_v)` - (64, 64).T @ (64, 32) = (64, 64) @ (64, 32) 7. Another `tl.dot(b_kg.T, b_v)` for the second K tile That's 7 matmuls of size (64, 64) @ (64, 32) per chunk. For 32 chunks: 224 matmuls per block. With 64 thread blocks (B=2, H=8, V_tiles=4 for BV=32), total matmuls = 224 * 64 = 14,336. Each matmul is 64*64*32*2 = 262K FLOPs. Total FLOPs = 14,336 * 262K = 3.76B FLOPs. At 200 TFLOPS, this is 0.019ms. But the actual time is 0.28ms, which is 15x slower. The overhead comes from: 1. Loading/storing data for each chunk 2. Kernel launch and synchronization overhead between chunks 3. Triton compiler not generating optimal code for SM120 For (2), since all chunks are in a single kernel, there's no kernel launch overhead between chunks. But there might be instruction scheduling overhead. For (3), Triton 3.6 might not fully optimize for Blackwell yet. Let me try a different approach: write the inter-chunk kernel in CUDA instead of Triton. This gives me more control over the memory access patterns and might avoid Triton's suboptimal code generation. Actually, let me first check if there are any obvious issues with my Triton kernel. One thing I notice is that I'm loading `b_glast1` and `b_glast2` using direct pointer arithmetic instead of `tl.load` with masks. Let me check if this is correct. ```python last_idx = t0 + BT - 1 p_glast1 = g_base + last_idx * H * K + tl.arange(0, 64) p_glast2 = g_base + last_idx * H * K + 64 + tl.arange(0, 64) b_glast1 = tl.load(p_glast1, mask=tl.arange(0, 64) < 64, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) b_glast2 = tl.load(p_glast2, mask=tl.arange(0, 64) < 64, other=0.0).to(tl.float32) ``` Wait, `g_base` is a pointer to the start of the g tensor. For a given (b, h), `g_base = g_ptr + (b * T * H + h) * K`. Then `g_base + last_idx * H * K + offset` points to `g[b, last_idx, h, offset]`. The stride in the T dimension is `H * K`, and in the K dimension is `1`. So this is correct. Another thing: in the state update, I compute: ```python b_kg = b_k * tl.exp(b_glast1[None, :] - b_g) ``` `b_glast1` has shape `(64,)`. `b_glast1[None, :]` has shape `(1, 64)`. `b_g` has shape `(64, 64)` (BT, BK). The subtraction broadcasts correctly. Then `b_S1 += tl.dot(b_kg.to(b_v.dtype).T, b_v.to(b_k.dtype))` `b_kg.T` has shape `(64, 64)` (BK, BT). `b_v` has shape `(64, 32)` (BT, BV). The dot gives `(64, 32)` which matches `b_S1`. Correct. Hmm, the kernel logic seems correct. The slowness is probably from Triton not generating optimal code. Let me try writing a CUDA kernel for the inter-chunk part. It will be more work but might be much faster. For the CUDA kernel, each block handles one (B, H, V_tile). I'll use shared memory for the state and for loading tiles. Actually, I realize there's another optimization I can try first. In the Triton kernel, I have a lot of `tl.dot` operations. Triton might be using more shared memory than necessary. Let me try explicitly specifying `num_stages=1` and see if that helps. I already do that. Let me also try using bf16 for the matmul inputs instead of fp32. Triton's `tl.dot` with bf16 inputs might use Tensor Cores more efficiently on Blackwell. Currently, I do: ```python b_v -= tl.dot(b_w, b_S1.to(b_w.dtype)) ``` Where `b_w` is loaded from the tensor (bf16) and `b_S1` is fp32. The `.to(b_w.dtype)` converts to bf16. So the matmul is in bf16. Wait, but `b_S1` is fp32 and I convert it to bf16 for the dot. The accumulation is still fp32 (Triton's default). This should use Tensor Cores. Let me check if the issue is that Triton is not using Tensor Cores for these small shapes. On some architectures, Tensor Cores have a minimum tile size. Actually, let me try running with `CUDA_VISIBLE_DEVICES=0` and checking ncu. But I don't have ncu installed. Let me check what's available.

import torch
import triton
import reference
import solution
import shapes

device = torch.device('cuda:0')
shape = shapes.SHAPES[1]
for k, v in shape.items():
    setattr(reference, k, v)

init_args = reference.get_init_inputs()
sol_model = solution.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()

torch.manual_seed(2026)
inputs = [t.to(device) if hasattr(t, 'to') else t for t in reference.get_inputs()]

# Warmup
with torch.no_grad():
    for _ in range(5):
        _ = sol_model(*inputs)

# Now run just the inter-chunk kernel with ncu
from solution import kda_inter_chunk_kernel

B, T, H, K, V = shape['B'], shape['T'], shape['H'], shape['K'], shape['V']
BT = shape['CHUNK_SIZE']
BV = 32

with torch.no_grad():
    qf = inputs[0].float() * (float(K) ** -0.5)
    kf = inputs[1].float()
    vf = inputs[2].float()
    gf = inputs[3].float()
    bf = inputs[4].float()
    
    B_, T_, H_, K_ = qf.shape
    V_ = vf.shape[-1]
    NT_ = T_ // BT
    
    qf = qf.view(B_, NT_, BT, H_, K_).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4).contiguous()
    kf = kf.view(B_, NT_, BT, H_, K_).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4).contiguous()
    vf = vf.view(B_, NT_, BT, H_, V_).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4).contiguous()
    gf = gf.view(B_, NT_, BT, H_, K_).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4).contiguous()
    bf = bf.view(B_, NT_, BT, H_).permute(0, 3, 1, 2).contiguous()
    
    gf = gf.cumsum(-2)
    
    kg = kf * gf.exp()
    kgn = kf * (-gf).exp()
    BHN = B_ * H_ * NT_
    kg_flat = kg.reshape(BHN, BT, K_)
    kgn_flat = kgn.reshape(BHN, BT, K_)
    A = torch.bmm(kg_flat, kgn_flat.transpose(-2, -1)).view(B_, H_, NT_, BT, BT)
    mask_upper = torch.triu(torch.ones(BT, BT, device=device, dtype=torch.bool), diagonal=0)
    A = A * bf[..., None]
    A = -A.masked_fill(mask_upper, 0)
    A_flat = A.view(BHN, BT, BT).contiguous()
    A_flat = solution._fwd_subst_mod.fwd_subst(A_flat)
    A = A_flat.view(B_, H_, NT_, BT, BT)
    I = torch.eye(BT, device=device, dtype=torch.float32)
    A = (A + I) * bf[..., None, :]
    w = torch.matmul(A, (gf.exp() * kf))
    u = torch.matmul(A, vf)
    qg = qf * gf.exp()
    qg_flat = qg.reshape(BHN, BT, K_)
    Aqk = torch.bmm(qg_flat, kgn_flat.transpose(-2, -1)).view(B_, H_, NT_, BT, BT)
    mask_strict = torch.triu(torch.ones(BT, BT, device=device, dtype=torch.bool), diagonal=1)
    Aqk = Aqk.masked_fill(mask_strict, 0)
    
    q_flat = qf.permute(0, 2, 3, 4, 1).reshape(B_, T_, H_, K_)
    k_flat = kf.permute(0, 2, 3, 4, 1).reshape(B_, T_, H_, K_)
    u_flat = u.permute(0, 2, 3, 4, 1).reshape(B_, T_, H_, V_)
    w_flat = w.permute(0, 2, 3, 4, 1).reshape(B_, T_, H_, K_)
    g_flat = gf.permute(0, 2, 3, 4, 1).reshape(B_, T_, H_, K_)
    Aqk_flat = Aqk.permute(0, 2, 3, 4, 1).reshape(B_, T_, H_, BT)
    o = torch.empty_like(inputs[2])
    
    scale = float(K_) ** -0.5
    
    grid = (triton.cdiv(V_, BV), B_ * H_)
    
    # Run many times for profiling
    for _ in range(100):
        kda_inter_chunk_kernel[grid](
            q_flat, k_flat, u_flat, w_flat, g_flat, Aqk_flat, o,
            B=B_, T=T_, H=H_, K=K_, V=V_, BT=BT, BV=BV, scale=scale,
            num_warps=4, num_stages=1,
        )
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    print('Done')

from solution import kda_inter_chunk_kernel

BT = shape['CHUNK_SIZE']
BV = 32

with torch.no_grad():
    qf = inputs[0].float() * (float(K) ** -0.5)
    kf = inputs[1].float()
    vf = inputs[2].float()
    gf = inputs[3].float()
    bf = inputs[4].float()
    
    B_, T_, H_, K_ = qf.shape
    V_ = vf.shape[-1]
    NT_ = T_ // BT
    
    qf = qf.view(B_, NT_, BT, H_, K_).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4).contiguous()
    kf = kf.view(B_, NT_, BT, H_, K_).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4).contiguous()
    vf = vf.view(B_, NT_, BT, H_, V_).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4).contiguous()
    gf = gf.view(B_, NT_, BT, H_, K_).permute(0, 3, 1, 2, 4).contiguous()
    bf = bf.view(B_, NT_, BT, H_).permute(0, 3, 1, 2).contiguous()
    
    gf = gf.cumsum(-2)
    
    kg = kf * gf.exp()
    kgn = kf * (-gf).exp()
    BHN = B_ * H_ * NT_
    kg_flat = kg.reshape(BHN, BT, K_)
    kgn_flat = kgn.reshape(BHN, BT, K_)

    mask_upper = torch.triu(torch.ones(BT, BT, device=device, dtype=torch.bool), diagonal=0)
    A = A * bf[..., None]
    A = -A.masked_fill(mask_upper, 0)
    A_flat = A.view(BHN, BT, BT).contiguous()
    A_flat = solution._fwd_subst_mod.fwd_subst(A_flat)
    A = A_flat.view(B_, H_, NT_, BT, BT)
    I = torch.eye(BT, device=device, dtype=torch.float32)
    A = (A + I) * bf[..., None, :]
    w = torch.matmul(A, (gf.exp() * kf))
    u = torch.matmul(A, vf)
    qg = qf * gf.exp()
    qg_flat = qg.reshape(BHN, BT, K_)
    Aqk = torch.bmm(qg_flat, kgn_flat.transpose(-2, -1)).view(B_, H_, NT_, BT, BT)
    mask_strict = torch.triu(torch.ones(BT, BT, device=device, dtype=torch.bool), diagonal=1)
    Aqk = Aqk.masked_fill(mask_strict, 0)
    
    q_flat = qf.permute(0, 2, 3, 4, 1).reshape(B_, T_, H_, K_)
    k_flat = kf.permute(0, 2, 3, 4, 1).reshape(B_, T_, H_, K_)
    u_flat = u.permute(0, 2, 3, 4, 1).reshape(B_, T_, H_, V_)
    w_flat = w.permute(0, 2, 3, 4, 1).reshape(B_, T_, H_, K_)
    g_flat = gf.permute(0, 2, 3, 4, 1).reshape(B_, T_, H_, K_)
    Aqk_flat = Aqk.permute(0, 2, 3, 4, 1).reshape(B_, T_, H_, BT)
    o = torch.empty_like(inputs[2])

import torch
import reference
import solution
import shapes

device = torch.device('cuda:0')
for shape_idx, shape in enumerate(shapes.SHAPES):
    for k, v in shape.items():
        setattr(reference, k, v)
    
    init_args = reference.get_init_inputs()
    sol_model = solution.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
    
    torch.manual_seed(2026)
    inputs = [t.to(device) if hasattr(t, 'to') else t for t in reference.get_inputs()]

    with torch.no_grad():
        for _ in range(10):
            _ = sol_model(*inputs)
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    
    # Time
    start = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    end = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    start.record()
    with torch.no_grad():
        for _ in range(50):
            _ = sol_model(*inputs)
    end.record()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    ms = start.elapsed_time(end) / 50
    
    # FLOPs
    B, T, H, K, V = shape['B'], shape['T'], shape['H'], shape['K'], shape['V']
    CHUNK_SIZE = shape['CHUNK_SIZE']
    flops = 4 * B * T * H * (K * V + CHUNK_SIZE * K + CHUNK_SIZE * V)
    tflops = flops / (ms * 1e-3) / 1e12
    peak_frac = tflops / 200.0
    
    print(f'Shape {shape_idx}: {ms:.3f} ms, {tflops:.2f} TFLOPS, peak_fraction={peak_frac:.4f}')

reasoning (8502 chars)
The performance improved slightly (from 0.71ms to 0.68ms for shape 1), but we're still at only 3.2% peak fraction. I need to be at 10%. Let me think about what's fundamentally limiting performance. The SOTA gets 0.2ms, which is 3.4x faster than my 0.68ms. The difference is that FLA has highly optimized Triton kernels for ALL parts, while I have: 1. PyTorch for data movement and some compute 2. A CUDA kernel for forward substitution 3. A Triton kernel for inter-chunk The inter-chunk Triton kernel alone takes ~0.25ms. The rest takes ~0.43ms. Even if I could make the inter-chunk instantaneous, I'd still be at 0.43ms, which is 2.1x slower than SOTA. So the bottleneck is actually the PyTorch ops for the intra-chunk part! Let me look at what those ops are and how to optimize them. For the intra-chunk part, the main ops are: 1. `view + permute + contiguous` for all inputs 2. `cumsum` 3. `kg = kf * gf.exp()` and `kgn = kf * (-gf).exp()` 4. `torch.bmm` for A 5. `masked_fill` 6. `fwd_subst` CUDA kernel 7. `matmul` for w and u 8. `bmm` for Aqk 9. `masked_fill` for Aqk 10. `permute + reshape` for Triton inputs The profiler earlier showed that `aten::copy_`, `aten::clone`, and elementwise kernels take a lot of time. Even with `.contiguous()`, there are still many intermediate tensors being created. One approach: write a Triton kernel for the entire intra-chunk computation! For each (B, H, chunk), the intra-chunk kernel would: 1. Load k, g, beta, v, q for the 64 tokens 2. Compute A = (k*exp(g)) @ (k*exp(-g)).T 3. Apply masking and beta 4. Forward substitution on A 5. Compute w = A @ (k*exp(g)) and u = A @ v 6. Compute Aqk = (q*exp(g)) @ (k*exp(-g)).T 7. Apply masking to Aqk 8. Store w, u, Aqk This is a lot of work for one kernel, but it would eliminate all the intermediate copies and kernel launch overhead. For K=128, V=128, C=64, each chunk has: - Loads: k(8K), g(8K fp32), beta(64), v(8K), q(8K) = ~40KB - Stores: w(8K), u(8K), Aqk(4K) = ~20KB - Compute: A(64×128@128×64) = 1M FLOPs, w(64×64@64×128) = 1M FLOPs, u(64×64@64×128) = 1M FLOPs, Aqk(64×128@128×64) = 1M FLOPs - Forward substitution: negligible with custom algorithm Total compute per chunk: ~4M FLOPs. For 512 chunks: ~2B FLOPs. At 200 TFLOPS: 0.01ms. Memory: 512 * 60KB = 30MB. At 1.8TB/s: 0.017ms. So theoretically, a fused intra-chunk kernel could run in ~0.02ms. Plus the inter-chunk kernel at ~0.25ms, total ~0.27ms. That's still above SOTA but much closer. Actually, the inter-chunk kernel should also be optimizable. FLA gets 0.2ms TOTAL, so their inter-chunk is probably ~0.05-0.1ms. For now, let me focus on writing a Triton kernel for the intra-chunk part to eliminate the PyTorch overhead. Actually, I just realized something. The `permute + contiguous` operations at the beginning are creating 5 large tensors. For B=2, T=2048, H=8, K=128: - qf: 2*2048*8*128*4 = 16.7MB - kf: 16.7MB - vf: 16.7MB - gf: 2*2048*8*128*4 = 16.7MB (already fp32) - bf: 2*2048*8*4 = 131KB That's ~67MB of copies just for the initial layout changes! At 1.8TB/s, that's 0.037ms. But in practice, with multiple copies and non-contiguous access, it might take more. Then the `cumsum` creates another 16.7MB tensor. Then `kg` and `kgn` create another 33.4MB. Then `A` creates a 4MB tensor. Then `w` and `u` create 16.7MB + 16.7MB. Then `Aqk` creates 4MB. Total intermediate memory: ~160MB per forward pass. That's a lot! For comparison, FLA's kernels probably use much less intermediate memory because they fuse operations. To really compete, I need to write more fused kernels. Let me write a Triton kernel for the intra-chunk part. For the intra-chunk kernel, I'll process one chunk per thread block. Grid: (NT, B*H). The kernel needs to: 1. Load k, g, beta into shared memory or registers 2. Compute A_kk = (k*exp(g)) @ (k*exp(-g)).T 3. Mask A_kk to strictly lower triangular 4. Do forward substitution on A_kk 5. Compute w = A_kk @ (k*exp(g)) and u = A_kk @ v 6. Compute Aqk = (q*exp(g)) @ (k*exp(-g)).T 7. Mask Aqk to lower triangular (including diagonal) 8. Store w, u, Aqk For K=128, I'll tile K into 64-wide blocks. For V=128, I'll tile V into 64-wide or 32-wide blocks. This is getting complex. Let me write a simplified version. Actually, for the forward substitution in Triton, I can use the same sequential loop approach but on a per-block basis. The 64×64 matrix can be stored in shared memory (16KB). Let me draft this kernel: ```python @triton.jit def kda_intra_chunk_kernel( q_ptr, k_ptr, v_ptr, g_ptr, beta_ptr, w_ptr, u_ptr, Aqk_ptr, B, T, H, K, V, BT, scale, BK: tl.constexpr, BV: tl.constexpr, ): i_t, i_bh = tl.program_id(0), tl.program_id(1) i_b, i_h = i_bh // H, i_bh % H t0 = i_t * BT # Load beta p_b = tl.make_block_ptr(beta_ptr + (i_b * T * H + i_h), (T,), (H,), (t0,), (BT,), (0,)) b_b = tl.load(p_b, boundary_check=(0,)).to(tl.float32) # Compute A_kk in shared memory # A = zeros(BT, BT) # For each k tile: # load k_tile, g_tile # kg = k * exp(g) # kgn = k * exp(-g) # A += kg @ kgn.T # But Triton doesn't have shared memory arrays easily... # Actually, we can accumulate A in registers by iterating over token pairs ``` This is tricky in Triton because we need to store a 64×64 matrix per block. With 64 threads, each thread could store 64 fp32 values = 256 bytes. That's 16KB total, which fits in shared memory. But Triton manages shared memory automatically. Actually, let me think about whether I can just compute A in a more Triton-friendly way. Since A[i,j] = sum_k kg[i,k] * kgn[j,k], this is exactly a matmul. I can compute it as: ```python b_A = tl.zeros((BT, BT), dtype=tl.float32) for i_k in range(tl.cdiv(K, BK)): p_k = tl.make_block_ptr(k_base, (T, K), (H*K, 1), (t0, i_k*BK), (BT, BK), (1, 0)) p_g = tl.make_block_ptr(g_base, (T, K), (H*K, 1), (t0, i_k*BK), (BT, BK), (1, 0)) b_k = tl.load(p_k, boundary_check=(0, 1)) b_g = tl.load(p_g, boundary_check=(0, 1)).to(tl.float32) b_kg = b_k * tl.exp(b_g) b_kgn = b_k * tl.exp(-b_g) b_A += tl.dot(b_kg, tl.trans(b_kgn)) ``` This computes the full A matrix. Then apply masking and beta: ```python o_i = tl.arange(0, BT) m_diag = o_i[:, None] > o_i[None, :] # strictly lower b_A = -tl.where(m_diag, b_A * b_b[:, None], 0.0) ``` Then forward substitution. This is the hard part in Triton. Let me see if I can do it with a sequential loop: ```python # Forward substitution in shared memory # Store b_A in a local array... but Triton doesn't support mutable arrays well ``` Actually, Triton does support sequential loops with `tl.store` and `tl.load` to shared memory. But for a 64×64 matrix, it's awkward. For simplicity, I can compute A in the Triton kernel, store it to global memory, then use my CUDA kernel for the forward substitution, then load it back for computing w and u. But this creates more memory traffic. Alternatively, I can do the forward substitution on the CPU since it's only 512 matrices of size 64×64. But 512*64 = 32K iterations, which would be slow on CPU. Wait, actually I can use the divide-and-conquer approach in PyTorch more efficiently. Earlier I had a batched DC approach that took 0.874ms for 512 matrices. But that was without trying to optimize it further. Let me try to write a more efficient PyTorch DC approach using only 2-3 bmm calls per level. Actually, for the DC approach, the main cost is the stacking and unstacking. Let me try a different formulation. For C=64, the matrix (I - A)^{-1} can be computed using the fact that A is strictly lower triangular. The DC formula is: For M = [T, 0; O, B] where T and B are lower triangular: M^{-1} = [T^{-1}, 0; -B^{-1} @ O @ T^{-1}, B^{-1}] We can compute this using `torch.block_diag` and `torch.matmul` on the appropriate slices. But this is still complex to vectorize efficiently. Let me try a completely different approach. What if I use the `torch.linalg.solve_triangular` function? ```python L = torch.tril(A, diagonal=-1) # strictly lower triangular I = torch.eye(BT, device=A.device, dtype=A.dtype).expand_as(A) # We want (I - L)^{-1} # This is equivalent to solving (I - L) @ X = I for X ``` But `solve_triangular` requires the matrix to be triangular, and I-L is unit lower triangular. We can use: ```python X = torch.linalg.solve_triangular(I - L, I, upper=False) ``` But this operates on each matrix in the batch. For 512 matrices of size 64×64, let me test its performance.

import torch
import time

C = 64
BHN = 512
A = torch.randn(BHN, C, C, device='cuda') * 0.1
mask = torch.tril(torch.ones(C, C, device='cuda'), diagonal=-1)
A = A * mask
I = torch.eye(C, device='cuda').expand_as(A)

# Test solve_triangular
start = time.time()
for _ in range(100):
    L = I - A
    X = torch.linalg.solve_triangular(L, I, upper=False)
torch.cuda.synchronize()
print(f'solve_triangular: {(time.time()-start)/100*1000:.3f} ms')

# Reference forward substitution
start = time.time()
for _ in range(100):
    ref = A.clone()
    for i in range(1, C):
        ref[:, i, :i] = ref[:, i, :i] + (ref[:, i, :, None] * ref[:, :, :i]).sum(-2)
    ref = ref + I
torch.cuda.synchronize()
print(f'fwd subst loop: {(time.time()-start)/100*1000:.3f} ms')

reasoning (3303 chars)
`solve_triangular` is 0.489ms, which is faster than the loop (1.291ms) but slower than my CUDA kernel (0.034ms). So my CUDA kernel is still the best for forward substitution. Now, the key insight is that the intra-chunk part without forward substitution takes ~0.2ms, and with my CUDA kernel it takes ~0.23ms. The inter-chunk kernel takes ~0.25ms. But there are also many copies and overhead operations taking ~0.2ms. So to get to SOTA performance, I need to: 1. Fuse the intra-chunk computations into fewer kernels 2. Optimize the inter-chunk kernel 3. Eliminate copies For step 1, I can write a Triton kernel that computes A, w, u, Aqk all in one go. But the forward substitution is hard in Triton. What if I keep my CUDA kernel for forward substitution, but wrap the rest in Triton? Actually, let me look at this from a different angle. The problem says I can use "CUTLASS CuTe on SM120 but Triton, CUDA C++ via load_inline, or inline PTX are also fine". What if I write the ENTIRE forward pass in a single CUDA kernel using load_inline? This would give me maximum control and eliminate all PyTorch overhead. For each (B, H, V_tile), the CUDA kernel would: 1. Initialize state S = zeros(K, V_tile) in shared memory 2. For each chunk i_t: a. Load q, k, v, g, beta for 64 tokens into shared memory b. Compute A_kk in shared memory c. Forward substitution on A_kk in shared memory d. Compute w, u, Aqk e. Compute output and update state f. Store output This is complex but feasible. Let me estimate the shared memory needed: - State S: K * V_tile = 128 * 32 = 4K fp32 = 16KB - For V_tile=32: - q tile: 64 * 64 = 4K bf16 = 8KB (per K tile, need 2 loads) - Actually, we can load directly from global memory for matmuls using CUDA's warp matrix multiply Wait, for CUDA C++, I can use the WMMA (Warp Matrix Multiply Accumulate) API or just regular shared memory + thread-level loops. For simplicity, let me write a CUDA kernel that: 1. Uses shared memory for the state S (128×32 fp32 = 16KB) 2. For each chunk, loads tiles into shared memory as needed 3. Uses simple thread-level matmuls (each thread computes one or more output elements) For a 64×64 @ 64×32 matmul: - Output is 64×32 = 2048 elements - With 128 threads, each thread computes 16 elements - Each element requires a dot product of length 64 - Total FLOPs per thread: 16 * 64 * 2 = 2048 FLOPs This is very manageable. And with 64 thread blocks (B=2, H=8, V_tiles=4 for V_tile=32), we have 64 * 128 = 8192 threads. Let me write this CUDA kernel. I'll need to handle: - Loading data from global memory in (B, T, H, K/V) layout - Computing A_kk = k_exp_g @ k_exp_neg_g.T - Forward substitution on A_kk - Computing w = A_kk @ k_exp_g - Computing u = A_kk @ v - Computing Aqk = q_exp_g @ k_exp_neg_g.T - Computing v_i = u_i - w_i @ S - Computing o_i = q_exp_g @ S + Aqk @ v_i - Updating S For the matmuls, I'll use a simple approach where threads cooperatively load tiles into shared memory and compute partial sums. Actually, this is a LOT of code. Let me see if there's a simpler approach. What if I use `torch.compile` with `fullgraph=True` and `mode='max-autotune'` on the entire forward method? Maybe the compiler can fuse more operations than I'm doing manually. Let me try compiling the forward method.

import torch
import reference
import solution
import shapes

device = torch.device('cuda:0')
shape = shapes.SHAPES[1]
for k, v in shape.items():
    setattr(reference, k, v)

init_args = reference.get_init_inputs()
sol_model = solution.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()

# Compile the forward method
compiled_model = torch.compile(sol_model, mode='max-autotune')

torch.manual_seed(2026)
inputs = [t.to(device) if hasattr(t, 'to') else t for t in reference.get_inputs()]

# Warmup
with torch.no_grad():
    for _ in range(10):
        _ = compiled_model(*inputs)

torch.cuda.synchronize()

# Time
start = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
end = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
start.record()
with torch.no_grad():
    for _ in range(50):
        _ = compiled_model(*inputs)
end.record()
torch.cuda.synchronize()
ms = start.elapsed_time(end) / 50

B, T, H, K, V = shape['B'], shape['T'], shape['H'], shape['K'], shape['V']
CHUNK_SIZE = shape['CHUNK_SIZE']
flops = 4 * B * T * H * (K * V + CHUNK_SIZE * K + CHUNK_SIZE * V)
tflops = flops / (ms * 1e-3) / 1e12
peak_frac = tflops / 200.0

print(f'Compiled: {ms:.3f} ms, {tflops:.2f} TFLOPS, peak_fraction={peak_frac:.4f}')

# Baseline
start.record()
with torch.no_grad():
    for _ in range(50):
        _ = sol_model(*inputs)
end.record()
torch.cuda.synchronize()
ms2 = start.elapsed_time(end) / 50
tflops2 = flops / (ms2 * 1e-3) / 1e12
peak_frac2 = tflops2 / 200.0
print(f'Eager: {ms2:.3f} ms, {tflops2:.2f} TFLOPS, peak_fraction={peak_frac2:.4f}')

import torch
import reference
import solution
import shapes

device = torch.device('cuda:0')
for shape_idx, shape in enumerate(shapes.SHAPES):
    for k, v in shape.items():
        setattr(reference, k, v)
    
    init_args = reference.get_init_inputs()
    sol_model = solution.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
    compiled_model = torch.compile(sol_model, mode='max-autotune')
    
    torch.manual_seed(2026)
    inputs = [t.to(device) if hasattr(t, 'to') else t for t in reference.get_inputs()]
    
    # Extensive warmup
    with torch.no_grad():
        for _ in range(20):
            _ = compiled_model(*inputs)
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    
    # Time
    start = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    end = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    start.record()
    with torch.no_grad():
        for _ in range(50):
            _ = compiled_model(*inputs)
    end.record()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    ms = start.elapsed_time(end) / 50
    
    # FLOPs
    B, T, H, K, V = shape['B'], shape['T'], shape['H'], shape['K'], shape['V']
    CHUNK_SIZE = shape['CHUNK_SIZE']
    flops = 4 * B * T * H * (K * V + CHUNK_SIZE * K + CHUNK_SIZE * V)
    tflops = flops / (ms * 1e-3) / 1e12
    peak_frac = tflops / 200.0
    
    print(f'Shape {shape_idx}: {ms:.3f} ms, {tflops:.2f} TFLOPS, peak_fraction={peak_frac:.4f}')
